Each A tile is 128 floats, loaded by 32 threads (4 floats each). Very efficient. 2. Loading B tiles: each B tile is reused for 128,016 M tiles. Total B loads = 8 × 180 = 1,440 B tiles per block of N tiles. Wait, this depends on how we organize the grid. If we have each block compute multiple M tiles for one N tile, then B is loaded once per K iteration and reused. This is efficient. If each block computes one M tile for one N tile, B is loaded many times. Better organization: each block handles multiple M tiles and one N tile. For example, a block with 8 warps can handle 8 different M tiles (each warp handles one M tile) for the same N tile. Then for each K iteration: - All 8 warps need the same B tile - B tile is broadcast loaded by all warps (though each warp loads its own fragment) - Each warp constructs its own A tile - Each warp does mma_sync This way, B is loaded once per warp but all warps use the same B. The B matrix is tiny (8×8 floats) and easily cached. Let me design the kernel: **Grid:** - x dimension: M tiles / warps_per_block. Let's say warps_per_block = 8. Then x = 128,016 / 8 = 16,002. - y dimension: N tiles = 8. **Block:** - 8 warps = 256 threads. **Each block:** - Handles 8 M tiles (128 output pixels) for 1 N tile (8 output channels). - For each K tile (180 iterations): - Each warp loads its own A fragment (16×8) from shared memory - Each warp loads its own B fragment (8×8) from global/shared memory - Each warp does mma_sync **Shared memory:** - A tiles: 8 warps × 16 × 8 floats = 1,024 floats = 4KB. Actually, each warp can construct its A tile in shared memory and load from there. If we allocate 128 floats per warp: 8 × 128 = 1,024 floats. - Or, we can have all warps construct their A tiles in shared memory simultaneously since they're for different M positions. Wait, constructing A tiles requires reading from global input memory. Each A tile for a warp corresponds to 16 specific output pixels. The 8 warps in a block need 8 different sets of 16 output pixels, which are 128 consecutive output pixels (if we arrange them consecutively in M). For M = 128 consecutive output pixels, the input patches overlap significantly. Maybe we can load a larger input region into shared memory and have all warps read from it? Actually, for M=128 output pixels and K_tile=8, each A tile needs: - 16 output pixels × 8 k values = 128 input values per warp - 8 warps × 128 = 1,024 values total But if the 128 output pixels are consecutive in global_m, their input patches overlap. For 128 output pixels along W dimension: - First pixel needs input cols w_start..w_start+8 - Last pixel needs input cols w_start+127..w_start+135 - Total span: w_start..w_start+135 (136 cols) - Rows: depends on whether 128 pixels span multiple rows. Since W_out=504, 128 pixels might be in the same row. If they're all in one row, the input region is 1 row × 136 cols per channel, for the 8 k values. But the 8 k values might span multiple channels! If K_tile is within one channel (e.g., k=0..7 which is c=0, kh=0, kw=0..7), then we need 1 channel, 5 rows (because kh can be 0..4 for different k tiles), and 136 cols. This shared memory analysis is getting complex. For simplicity, let me just have each warp load its own A tile directly from global memory into its registers/shared memory. Since each A tile is small (128 floats) and the global memory reads are cached, this should be fine. Wait, but `wmma::load_matrix_sync` for A expects a pointer to contiguous memory (row-major 16×8). We can have each warp use a small shared memory buffer (128 floats) for this. Each warp fills its buffer from global memory, then loads into the fragment. For 8 warps, we need 8 × 128 × 4 = 4KB of shared memory. Very little! And for B: each warp loads a B fragment. The B matrix is [K=1440][N=64] col-major. For a given N tile and K tile, B tile is at `weight + n_idx * K + k_start`. Since all warps need the same B tile, they all load from the same address. But wmma load_matrix_sync distributes elements to threads, so all threads in the warp participate in the load. For B, we could load into shared memory once per block and then each warp does load_matrix_sync from shared memory. Or each warp can load directly from global memory. Since the B tile is only 64 floats and cached, direct global load is fine. Actually, the simplest approach is to not use shared memory for B at all, and let each warp load B directly from global memory via wmma::load_matrix_sync. For A, we do need a shared memory buffer because the data is implicit (needs to be gathered from the input feature map). But we could also load directly from global memory if we pre-gather into a temporary buffer. With implicit indexing, we need to gather first. Wait, actually `wmma::load_matrix_sync` requires a linear memory layout. We can't use it with implicit indexing. So we do need to construct the A tile in memory first (shared memory is fastest). Let me design the kernel step by step: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_wmma_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int KH, int KW, int H_out, int W_out ) { using namespace nvcuda::wmma; const int WMMA_M = 16; const int WMMA_N = 8; const int WMMA_K = 8; const int K = C_in * KH * KW; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; int n_warps = blockDim.x / 32; // Each block computes n_warps M-tiles × 1 N-tile int m_tile_base = (blockIdx.x * n_warps + warp_id) * WMMA_M; int n_tile = blockIdx.y; int n_idx = n_tile * WMMA_N; // Shared memory for A tiles extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* a_smem = smem + warp_id * (WMMA_M * WMMA_K); // 128 floats per warp // Accumulator fragment<accumulator, WMMA_M, WMMA_N, WMMA_K, float> acc_frag; fill_fragment(acc_frag, 0.0f); fragment<matrix_a, WMMA_M, WMMA_N, WMMA_K, precision::tf32, row_major> a_frag; fragment<matrix_b, WMMA_M, WMMA_N, WMMA_K, precision::tf32, col_major> b_frag; for (int k_start = 0; k_start < K; k_start += WMMA_K) { // Construct A tile in shared memory: M×K = 16×8 // Row i: output pixel m_tile_base + i // Col j: k index = k_start + j for (int idx = lane_id; idx < WMMA_M * WMMA_K; idx += 32) { int row = idx / WMMA_K; int col = idx % WMMA_K; int global_m = m_tile_base + row; int global_k = k_start + col; // Decompose global_m int b = global_m / (H_out * W_out); int rem = global_m % (H_out * W_out); int h_out = rem / W_out; int w_out = rem % W_out; // Decompose global_k int c = global_k / (KH * KW); int rem2 = global_k % (KH * KW); int kh = rem2 / KW; int kw = rem2 % KW; int h_in = h_out + kh; int w_in = w_out + kw; float val = 0.0f; if (b < B && h_in < H && w_in < W && c < C_in) { val = input[((b * C_in + c) * H + h_in) * W + w_in]; } a_smem[idx] = val; } __syncwarp(); // Load A fragment wmma::load_matrix_sync(a_frag, a_smem, WMMA_K); // Load B fragment from global memory const float* b_ptr = weight + (n_idx * K + k_start); wmma::load_matrix_sync(b_frag, b_ptr, K); // MMA wmma::mma_sync(acc_frag, a_frag, b_frag, acc_frag); __syncwarp(); } // Store result float* c_smem = smem + warp_id * (WMMA_M * WMMA_N); wmma::store_matrix_sync(c_smem, acc_frag, WMMA_N, mem_row_major); for (int idx = lane_id; idx < WMMA_M * WMMA_N; idx += 32) { int row = idx / WMMA_N; int col = idx % WMMA_N; int global_m = m_tile_base + row; int global_n = n_idx + col; int b = global_m / (H_out * W_out); int rem = global_m % (H_out * W_out); int h_out = rem / W_out; int w_out = rem % W_out; int oc = global_n; if (b < B && oc < C_out && h_out < H_out && w_out < W_out) { output[((b * C_out + oc) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = c_smem[idx]; } } } ``` Now, there's an important issue with the B matrix layout. In the code above, I use `const float* b_ptr = weight + (n_idx * K + k_start)`. But `weight` is [C_out, C_in, KH, KW]. For a given n_idx and k_start, does `weight + n_idx * K + k_start` point to the correct float? `weight[n_idx, c, kh, kw]` where `c*KH*KW + kh*KW + kw = k_start`. Yes! Because: `weight[n_idx * C_in * KH * KW + k_start]` = `weight[n_idx * K + k_start]` where `k_start = c*KH*KW + kh*KW + kw`. So this is correct! The weight tensor in PyTorch is stored as [C_out][C_in][KH][KW], and when we index as `weight[n_idx * K + k]`, we're effectively treating it as a 2D matrix of shape [C_out, K] row-major. For WMMA's col_major B matrix, element (k, n) is at `&B[0][0] + k + n * K`. If we set `B = weight` viewed as [C_out][K] row-major, then: - `B[n][k]` = `weight[n * K + k]` - But col_major needs `B[k][n]` at `base + k + n * K` - This is exactly the same address! Because `base + k + n * K` corresponds to `B[n][k]` in row-major if B is [N][K] row-major. Wait, let me double check this. For a matrix with dimensions K rows × N columns: - Col-major: element (k, n) is at `base + k + n * K` - Row-major: element (n, k) is at `base + k + n * K` for a [N][K] matrix So a [N][K] row-major matrix is exactly the same memory layout as a [K][N] col-major matrix! And our weight tensor is [C_out][C_in][KH][KW] which can be reshaped to [C_out][K] row-major. This is exactly the col-major layout needed for the B matrix! So `b_ptr = weight + n_idx * K + k_start` is correct for loading a K_tile×N_tile submatrix in col_major format. Great! Now for the grid dimensions: - M_tiles = (B * H_out * W_out + WMMA_M - 1) / WMMA_M = (8 * 508 * 504 + 15) / 16 = (2,048,256 + 15) / 16 = 128,017 (due to not being perfectly divisible) - Wait, 2,048,256 / 16 = 128,016 exactly! Because 2,048,256 is even and 2,048,256 / 16 = 128,016. Let me verify: 128,016 * 16 = 2,048,256. Yes! - N_tiles = (64 + 7) / 8 = 8 exactly. - If we use 8 warps per block, each block handles 8 M tiles. - M tiles per block = 8. - Grid.x = 128,016 / 8 = 16,002. - Grid.y = 8. - Total blocks = 16,002 * 8 = 128,016. Block size = 8 * 32 = 256 threads. Shared memory per block: - A tiles: 8 warps * 128 floats = 1,024 floats = 4KB - C tiles: 8 warps * 128 floats = 1,024 floats = 4KB - We can overlap A and C if we want, but C is only used at the end. - Total = ~8KB. With 8KB shared memory per block and 227KB per SM, we can have up to 28 blocks per SM (limited by shared memory). But we're limited to 32 blocks per SM anyway. And 256 threads per block means max 2048/256 = 8 blocks per SM from thread limit. So 8 blocks per SM is the limit from threads: 8 × 256 = 2,048 threads = max per SM. Total active blocks = 132 SMs × 8 = 1,056 blocks. Total blocks = 128,016. Waves = 128,016 / 1,056 = 121.2 waves. Each block does 8 M tiles × 1 N tile × 180 K tiles = 1,440 mma operations. Each mma_sync(m16n8k8) does 1,024 FMAs. Total FMAs per block = 1,440 × 1,024 = 1,474,560. Total FLOPs per block = 2,949,120. At 500 TFLOPS Tensor Core across the GPU: total blocks can execute at 128,016 / (132 * 8) = 121 waves. If each wave takes T seconds: T * 121 = total time. FLOPs per wave = 1,056 blocks * 2,949,120 FLOPs = 3.114e9 FLOPs. At 500 TFLOPS: 3.114e9 / 500e12 = 6.23 microseconds per wave. Total time = 121 * 6.23us = 754 microseconds = 0.754 ms. This is a rough estimate, but it shows the kernel should be competitive with cuDNN. Of course, there are overheads: 1. Computing global_m decomposition for each of the 128 elements per A tile 2. Here's the complete kernel code I'll start with: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <mma.h> using namespace nvcuda; torch::Tensor conv2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, int64_t B, int64_t C_in, int64_t H, int64_t W, int64_t C_out, int64_t KH, int64_t KW, int64_t H_out, int64_t W_out ) { auto output = torch::empty({B, C_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); const int WMMA_M = 16; const int WMMA_N = 8; const int WMMA_K = 8; const int K = C_in * KH * KW; int M = B * H_out * W_out; int m_tiles = (M + WMMA_M - 1) / WMMA_M; int n_tiles = (C_out + WMMA_N - 1) / WMMA_N; Let me write the `ModelNew` class: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, groups=1, bias=False): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=groups, bias=bias) def forward(self, x): B, C, H, W = x.shape weight = self.conv2d.weight out_channels, in_channels, KH, KW = weight.shape H_out = H - KH + 1 W_out = W - KW + 1 return conv2d_forward(x, weight, B, C, H, W, out_channels, KH, KW, H_out, W_out) ``` This is clean and simple. Let me now think about potential issues with the WMMA kernel: 1. **Compilation errors**: `precision::tf32` might not be available or might require different syntax. Since TF32 has 10 mantissa bits, the error should be well within 1e-4 for random data unless there are specific pathological cases. For random data in [0, 1], conv outputs might be in the range [0, 32*5*9] = [0, 1440]. With TF32, relative error is about 2^-10 = 0.001 absolute at unit scale, but values accumulate over 1,440 terms. Wait, TF32 rounds each product to 10 mantissa bits. The product of two [0,1] random numbers is in [0,1]. Rounding a [0,1] value to TF32 gives maximum absolute error of about 2^-10 ≈ 0.001. When summing 1,440 such rounded products, the accumulated error depends on: - If all errors are systematic in the same direction, max error ≈ 1,440 * 0.001 = 1.44 - If errors are random, stddev ≈ sqrt(1,440) * 0.001 / sqrt(3) ≈ 0.022 For random data, we expect the central limit theorem to give errors around a few times 0.022, maybe up to 0.1 or so. But with TF32, the rounding is the same for each multiply. The add order matters slightly. For our kernel, with WMMA, the accumulation order is determined by the hardware and might differ from cuDNN. However, since both use TF32, the difference should be small. If the difference turns out to be larger than 1e-4, we might need to compute in higher precision. But let's see if it matches first. Actually, I just realized something important. In our kernel, we use `wmma::precision::tf32` for the A and B fragments, but the actual values stored in memory are FP32. `wmma::load_matrix_sync` with `precision::tf32` reads FP32 values and converts them to TF32 internally for the MMA operation. The result is accumulated in FP32 in the accumulator fragment. So our kernel computes: C = sum(A_tf32 * B_tf32) in FP32 accumulation. cuDNN with TF32 computes: C = sum(A_tf32 * B_tf32) in FP32 or FP32 accumulation. This should be very close. But the difference might come from: 1. How TF32 conversion is done (round-to-nearest even) 2. Operation ordering Since the tolerance is 1e-4, and typical conv outputs are in the range [0, 1440] for our specific random inputs, 1e-4 relative tolerance means absolute tolerance up to 0.144. That's quite generous. For values near 0, absolute tolerance of 1e-4. So small values must match closely. I think this should be fine. But if not, we can always switch to a direct FP32 kernel. Let me assemble the code and try to compile. One more thing: I notice that for the forward declaration in C++, I just declared it as `void conv2d_wmma_kernel(...)`. But in CUDA, it's `__global__ void conv2d_wmma_kernel(...)`. The C++ compiler might not understand `__global__`, which is why I declared it without it. This should work since nvcc compiles the CUDA source and generates the appropriate symbols. OK, here's my full code:

reasoning
The compilation failed for two reasons: 1. WMMA on sm_90 only supports certain layouts. The combination M=16, N=8, K=8 with tf32 doesn't exist in wmma. For H100 (sm_90), I should probably use Hopper-specific tensor core APIs (wgmma) or just skip tensor cores and use a well-optimized CUDA kernel with shared memory and vectorized loads. Let me reconsider. For a standard conv2d on H100, the best approach is often a direct CUDA kernel that treats it as an im2col GEMM, or even better, use cuDNN/cuBLAS GEMM under the hood. But the point of the exercise is to write a custom CUDA kernel that beats PyTorch. Actually, let me think about this more carefully: - Input: B=8, C=32, H=W=512 - Kernel: C_out=64, KH=5, KW=9 - Output: H_out=508, W_out=504 - M = 8*508*504 = 2,048,256 - N = 64 - K = 32*5*9 = 1,440 This is a GEMM-like problem. Since N=64 is very small, a standard cublas GEMM call with im2col might not be optimal. However, PyTorch's own convolution implementation already uses cuDNN which is highly optimized. Instead, I could try to write a direct convolution kernel that is well tuned. But beating cuDNN for conv2d on H100 is very hard. Wait, let me re-read the problem. The parameters say: ``` batch_size = 8 in_channels = 32 out_channels = 64 kernel_size = (5, 9) width = 512 height = 512 ``` And padding=0, stride=1, dilation=1. So H_out = 512-5+1 = 508, W_out = 512-9+1 = 504. One key insight: N=64 is small. A well-written kernel that handles small output channels efficiently using float4 vectorization and shared memory might beat cuDNN by avoiding the overhead of im2col + GEMM. Let me write a simpler, well-optimized direct CUDA kernel: Strategy: - Each thread block handles a tile of output - Use shared memory for input patches - Vectorized loads (float4) - Unroll loops where possible Actually, a simpler approach that often works well on H100: simply use `aten::cudnn_convolution` or write a kernel that is similar to a direct convolution but optimized for the exact problem sizes. Wait, since the problem size is fixed (well, fixed for the test), I can write a kernel specifically tuned for C_in=32, C_out=64, KH=5, KW=9. For direct convolution, each output pixel requires 32*5*9 = 1,440 multiply-adds. For 2M output pixels, that's ~2.9 GMACs (or 5.9 GFLOPs at fp32). At 67 TFLOPS (H100 FP32), the compute time would be ~0.09 ms. The DRAM bandwidth is 3.35 TB/s. Reading input (8*32*512*512*4 = 268MB), weights (64*32*5*9*4 = 368KB), and writing output (8*64*508*504*4 = 525MB) - actually the input and output sizes are large. Wait, but with cache reuse, the weights can stay in L2, and inputs are reused. Let me compute arithmetic intensity: - FLOPs: B * C_out * H_out * W_out * 2 * C_in * KH * KW = 8 * 64 * 508 * 504 * 2 * 32 * 5 * 9 ≈ 3.76e10 FLOPs = 37.6 GFLOPs - Memory traffic (naive): Input 8*32*512*512*4 = 268MB, Weights 64*32*5*9*4 = 368KB, Output 8*64*508*504*4 = 525MB. Total ~793MB. - AI = 37.6 GFLOPs / 0.793 GB = 47 FLOPs/B. At 3.35 TB/s bandwidth, memory-bound at ~95 GFLOPS, but with proper caching it could be better. Actually, 37.6 GFLOPs of compute at 67 TFLOPS = 0.56 ms. Memory bandwidth bound at 3.35 TB/s for 793MB = 0.24 ms. So it's compute-bound. Let me think about how to write this kernel efficiently. One approach: Use the CUTLASS-like approach with warp-specialization, but that's complex. A simpler approach that can work well: - Tile in B*H*W_out and C_out - Each warp computes a tile of output - Load input into shared memory cooperatively - Use vectorized loads (float4) - Unroll the KH and KW loops Actually, let me think more practically. PyTorch uses cuDNN's implicit GEMM or direct convolution algorithms. These are very highly optimized. But for this moderate-sized convolution with small C_in and C_out, a handwritten kernel might sometimes win if it avoids cuDNN overhead. Let me try a different strategy: use `torch.nn.functional.conv2d` but with a trick? No, that would be the reference. What if I treat this as an unrolled GEMM and use a custom CUTLASS-like kernel? Or just call cuBLAS after doing im2col in a CUDA kernel? Actually, the standard approach for custom conv kernels is: 1. Im2col in CUDA to expand input 2. cublas GEMM But for H100, cuBLAS GEMM is very fast. Let me check: M=2,048,256, N=64, K=1,440. If we do im2col + GEMM: - Im2col: need to write 2,048,256 * 1,440 * 4 bytes ≈ 11.8 GB. That's a lot of writes! - GEMM: M large, N small (64), K moderate (1440). A GEMM of (2Mx64) @ (64x1440) -> well, actually GEMM would be (2Mx1440) x (1440x64). This is a very tall-skinny GEMM. cublas might not handle this optimally. Actually, wait - for im2col + GEMM, the matrix A would be (M=2,048,256, K=1,440) and matrix B would be (K=1,440, N=64). cublas is pretty good at tall-skinny GEMMs. But 11.8 GB of im2col writes at 3.35 TB/s = 3.5 ms just for im2col! That would be very slow. So the direct convolution approach is better. Let me write a direct convolution kernel. Actually, wait - PyTorch's cuDNN might already use implicit GEMM which fuses im2col with GEMM, or it might use a direct convolution algorithm. We want to beat it. For H100, let me try a simple but well-optimized direct convolution kernel with vectorized loads and shared memory. Here's my plan for the kernel: - Block dimensions: 16x16 threads = 256 threads - Tile dimensions: each block computes output for some number of output channels and spatial positions - Since C_in=32, I can load all input channels into shared memory - Since KH=5, KW=9, I need to load a tile of input plus halo - Vectorize loads with float4 (4 contiguous elements in width) Wait, C_in=32 = 8 float4s. For each input channel, I load a tile of width. Let me think about a specific tiling scheme: - Each block handles 8 output channels (since C_out=64, we need 8 blocks in C_out dim) and some spatial tile - Actually, let me handle multiple output channels per warp/thread to amortize input loads Alternative tiling: - Block size: 256 threads - Each block computes a 16x16 tile of output pixels (in the H_out x W_out plane) for 8 output channels at once - This means 16*16*8 = 2,048 output elements per block - Each thread computes 2,048 / 256 = 8 output elements... but wait, actually 2,048 elements for 8 output channels means each thread handles 8 outputs if we perfectly parallelize. Let me simplify: - Each thread computes one output pixel for one output channel - Block: 16x16 output channels... no wait. Let's do this: - Grid in (block_out_h, block_out_w, block_c_out) - Each block has 16x16 threads - Each thread computes one (h, w) position for one output channel - We can have each thread compute multiple output channels by looping Actually, let me think of a classic direct conv approach: - Block computes an output tile of THxTW for TCo output channels - Threads load input tile of (TH+KH-1) x (TW+KW-1) x C_in into shared memory - Then each thread computes its outputs by reading from shared memory and weights from global/L2 For C_in=32, KH=5, KW=9: - Input shared memory per block for a 16x16 output tile: (16+4)*(16+8)*32 floats = 20*24*32*4 = 61,440 bytes. That's reasonable (within 48KB or even 96KB on H100). - We can use larger tiles to better amortize loads. For example, 32x32 output: (36*40*32*4) = 184KB - too large for shared memory. What about a 16x16 output tile with 61KB shared memory - that's fine, even for pre-H100. But wait, we also need to compute for C_out=64. We can either: 1. Have multiple blockIdx.z for output channels, each block reusing the same input tile 2. Have each block compute multiple output channels Option 2 is better if we can fit it. Let's have each block compute 4 output channels (we can use 4 warps, each warp computes one output channel for the 16x16 tile, or distribute differently). Actually, on H100, shared memory is 228KB per SM, but max 227KB per block. We can afford more. Let me try: - Block: 256 threads organized as (16, 16) - Block computes 16x16 output in H-W for 4 output channels - Total output per block: 16*16*4 = 1,024 elements - Shared memory for input: (16+4)*(16+8)*32 = 20*24*32 = 15,360 floats = 61,440 bytes - Each thread computes 4 output elements (one for each output channel) Alternatively, for better occupancy: - Block: 128 threads organized as (16, 8) - Block computes 16x16 output in H-W for 2 output channels - Shared memory: still 61KB - This allows 2 blocks per SM (122KB shared), or even 3 with dynamic partitioning Actually, on H100, a good strategy is to use warp-level tile sizes. With 128 threads (4 warps), we can assign: - Each warp computes 16x16 output elements for 1 output channel - Total 4 output channels per block - 128 threads, each thread computes (16*16)/32 = 8 outputs for 1 channel, or 2 outputs for 4 channels Hmm, let me try a simpler but more vectorized approach: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int KH, int KW, int H_out, int W_out ) { // Each thread computes one output element for one output channel int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_out = B * C_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total_out) return; int out_w = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int out_h = tmp % H_out; tmp = tmp / H_out; int out_c = tmp % C_out; int out_b = tmp / C_out; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { float in_val = input[((out_b * C_in + c) * H + (out_h + kh)) * W + (out_w + kw)]; float w_val = weight[((out_c * C_in + c) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } output[idx] = acc; } ``` This simple kernel would be terrible because each thread does uncoalesced memory accesses and doesn't share input data. For direct conv, shared memory is key. Let me design with shared memory: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_direct_kernel(...) { // Block dimensions: (16, 16) threads // Each block computes 16x16 outputs in H_out x W_out for a specific batch and output channel range const int TILE_H = 16; const int TILE_W = 16; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * 8; // 8 output channels per block (for C_out=64, need 8 blocks in y) int h_tile = blockIdx.x / ((W_out + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W); int w_tile = blockIdx.x % ((W_out + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W); int h_out = h_tile * TILE_H + threadIdx.y; int w_out = w_tile * TILE_W + threadIdx.x; if (h_out >= H_out || w_out >= W_out) return; // Load input tile into shared memory __shared__ float s_input[TILE_H + KH - 1][TILE_W + KW - 1][C_in]; // Each thread loads some portion int in_h_start = h_tile * TILE_H; int in_w_start = w_tile * TILE_W; for (int idx = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; idx < (TILE_H + KH - 1) * (TILE_W + KW - 1) * C_in; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { // compute c, h, w from idx ... s_input[h][w][c] = input[...]; } __syncthreads(); // Now compute output for (int oc = 0; oc < 8; ++oc) { int c_out = c_out_base + oc; if (c_out >= C_out) break; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { float in_val = s_input[threadIdx.y + kh][threadIdx.x + kw][c]; float w_val = weight[((c_out * C_in + c) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } output[((b * C_out + c_out) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = sum; } } ``` This is much better but has issues: 1. `(TILE_H + KH - 1) * (TILE_W + KW - 1) * C_in` = 20*24*32 = 15,360 floats. With 256 threads, each thread loads 60 floats (taking 60*4 = 240 bytes per thread, or about 15 memory instructions per thread with float4). 2. The shared memory accesses in the compute phase are not perfectly coalesced because of the [c][h][w] vs [h][w][c] ordering. Let me optimize memory layout: - Store shared memory as [C_in][TILE_H+KH-1][TILE_W+KW-1] to have channel-major. Then when computing, for a fixed output channel, we iterate over channels, and within each channel we access a 2D spatial tile. If we keep it as [h][w][c], when accessing [threadIdx.y + kh][threadIdx.x + kw][c], consecutive threads (same threadIdx.x or y) might have bank conflicts. Actually, the compute phase accesses `s_input[threadIdx.y + kh][threadIdx.x + kw][c]`. If stored as [h][w][c], with dim1=20, dim2=24, dim3=32: - consecutive threads in x access different w, same h, same c: addresses differ by 32*sizeof(float) = 128 bytes. 128 / 32 (banks) = 4. So threads in a warp with consecutive threadIdx.x would access banks 0, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, 28, 0, 4, ... for the first 8 threads, then banks 1, 5, ... for next 8, etc. This might be okay-ish. But let's consider `__shared__ float s_input[C_in][TILE_H + KH - 1][TILE_W + KW - 1]`; - Access: `s_input[c][threadIdx.y + kh][threadIdx.x + kw]` - Consecutive threads in x (consecutive threadIdx.x) access same c, same h, consecutive w. Since w is the fastest varying dimension, consecutive threads access consecutive floats -> broadcast/vector load, no bank conflicts! - In a warp of 32 threads with threadIdx.x=0..15 and threadIdx.y=0..1 (or some arrangement), we get good access patterns. Wait, but threads are usually arranged in x-major order within a warp. If threadIdx ranges from (0..15, 0..15), the first warp has threadIdx.x=0..15 for threadIdx.y=0 (16 threads), then threadIdx.x=0..15 for threadIdx.y=1 (another 16 threads). So in a warp, we have 16 threads accessing (y, w=0..15) and 16 threads accessing (y+1, w=0..15). If we access `s_input[c][threadIdx.y + kh][threadIdx.x + kw]`, all threads in the warp access same c, and within each half-warp, consecutive w. The two halves have different h values but same w range. This would still have good memory access (no bank conflicts). Actually, even better - if we access `s_input[c][threadIdx.y + kh][threadIdx.x + kw]`, for fixed c, kh, kw, each thread accesses a unique element. In a warp, there are 32 unique accesses. With [c][h][w] layout and w up to 24, thread (y, x) accesses index [c][y+kh][x+kw]. The offset from base is (y+kh)*24 + (x+kw). For the first 16 threads (y=0, x=0..15): offset = kh*24 + kw + x. These are 16 consecutive integers -> no bank conflicts. For next 16 threads (y=1, x=0..15): offset = (1+kh)*24 + kw + x = 24 + kh*24 + kw + x. The first 16 threads use banks 0..15 (possibly wrapping around), and next 16 use banks offset by 24 mod 32. 24 mod 32 = 24, so first element of second half is bank 24, then 25, 26, 27, 28, 29, 30, 31, 0, 1, ... for the last 8. So first half: banks 0-15, second half: banks 24-31 then 0-7. There IS overlap! Banks 0-7 are accessed by both halves. BANK CONFLICTS! To fix this, we can pad the w dimension to 32 or use a swizzled layout. A simpler fix: pad [C_in][TILE_H+KH-1][TILE_W+KW-1 + PADDING] where padding ensures the stride between h rows is a multiple of 32 (number of banks), or at least odd/even to distribute better. If TILE_W+KW-1 = 24, and we pad to 32: [32][32] layout for h,w. Then offset = (y+kh)*32 + (x+kw). First half: offsets 0..15 + base. Second half: offsets 32..47 + base. Banks: first half banks 0..15, second half banks 0..15 (since 32 mod 32 = 0). Still conflicts! If we pad to 33: offsets first half: 0..15, second half: 33..48. Banks: first half 0..15, second half 1..16. Conflict on banks 1..15. If we pad to 31: first half banks 0..15, second half banks 31, 0..14. Conflict on 0..14. If we pad to 25: first half: 0..15, second half: 25..40. Banks first half: 0..15, second half: 25..31, 0..8. Conflicts on 0..8. What if we use a thread layout where each warp handles 32 consecutive values in some dimension? If tile_w = 16, we can't have 32 consecutive in w. Alternative approach: assign each warp to compute one row of the output (16 elements in w), and have 2 warps for 2 different h values, but also different c_out? That doesn't help for input reads. Alternative: have each thread compute multiple output channels for the same spatial position. Then threads in a warp all access the SAME input element simultaneously. On H100, shared memory broadcasts can handle this efficiently (no bank conflicts for same address). YES! This is the key insight! If we restructure: - Each block handles a tile in space (say 16x16) - Each thread computes ALL (or multiple) output channels for its spatial position - During the computation, for a given (c_in, kh, kw), all threads in a warp need the same input value (since they have different c_out but same spatial position). Wait, no - they have different spatial positions. But if we have each warp compute for different spatial positions but same c_out, they need different input values. Wait, let me reconsider: if 32 threads in a warp all compute the same spatial position but different c_out, they all need the SAME input values. For each (c_in, kh, kw), all 32 threads load the same s_input[c_in][h][w]. This is a broadcast - perfect, no bank conflicts! And each thread uses a different weight. Weights should be read from constant memory or shared memory too. This is brilliant for the compute phase! Let's structure it this way: - Block: 32 threads (1 warp) or 256 threads (8 warps) - Each warp computes 1 spatial position (or a small tile) for 32 output channels - Wait, but C_out = 64. We could have each warp compute for 2 spatial positions x 32 output channels, or 1 spatial position x 64 output channels. Actually, for 256 threads = 8 warps: - Option A: Each warp handles 32 output channels for 1 spatial position. 8 warps handle 8 spatial positions, each computing all 32 of their assigned output channels. - Block computes 8 spatial positions with all 64 output channels... wait, with 8 warps and C_out=64, we need 2 sets of 32. So each spatial position is handled by 2 warps (one for channels 0-31, one for channels 32-63). Total spatial positions per block: 8 warps / 2 = 4 spatial positions, each with all 64 output channels. Wait, but 4 spatial positions is very little compute per block (4*64 = 256 MACs). We need much more. Alternative: each warp handles 8 spatial positions x 8 output channels. Then 8 warps handle 64 spatial positions x 64 output channels. But then input reuse within a warp is poor. Let me think about what maximizes both input reuse and parallelism. Actually, the broadcast property is great but we need enough spatial positions to fill the block. Here's another idea: - Each warp computes a 4x4 tile of output (16 positions) for 4 output channels. - 8 warps compute: 16 * 4 * 8 = 512 output elements per block. - But for input reuse, within a warp, all 32 threads compute different spatial positions (so they need different inputs). No broadcast benefit. What if we have each warp compute 2 spatial positions (2 threads cooperate) for 32 output channels? - 32 threads in warp: 16 pairs, each pair computes 1 spatial position for 32 output channels. Actually, each pair could compute the same spatial position for different output channels. No, 32 threads for 32 output channels means 1 thread per output channel per spatial position. That gives us 1 spatial position per warp with all 32 output channels computed. With 2 warps we get 1 spatial position with all 64 channels. With 8 warps we get 4 spatial positions with all 64 channels. Still small. What about having a warp compute 1 spatial position for 32 output channels, but the spatial position is a vector of 4 adjacent elements? No, each channel is a scalar. Hmm, the problem is C_out=64 is quite small. For spatial parallelism, we have 508*504 ≈ 256K positions per batch, times 8 batches = 2M total spatial positions. Each needs 64 output values. Let's have each block compute a 16x16 spatial tile for 2 output channels (32 channels total? No, 2 channels). Then we need 32 blocks in the channel dimension. Actually, a standard approach for this: launch with grid where: - blockIdx.x covers spatial positions (B * H_out * W_out / tile_size) - blockIdx.y covers output channels (C_out / channels_per_block) If each block computes 256 spatial positions (e.g., 16x16) for 2 output channels: - Block size 256 threads, each thread computes 2 outputs - Shared memory for input tile: 20*24*32*4 = 61KB - Each block reads 61KB from global memory to shared, then each thread does 2 * 32 * 5 * 9 = 2,880 FMAs - Total FMAs per block: 256 * 2,880 = 737,280 FMAs - Arithmetic intensity: 737,280 FMAs / 61KB = 12 FMAs/byte. At 3.35 TB/s this gives 40 TFLOPS. H100 FP32 is 67 TFLOPS, so we're memory-bound but not terrible. With L2 cache reusing weights, it could be much better because weights are only read once globally but used many times. Actually, weights for 2 output channels: 2 * 32 * 5 * 9 = 2,880 floats = 11.5KB. Total memory read ~73KB (input + weights). AI = 737K / 73K = 10. Still not great. Wait, we need much better. Let me compute more carefully. For direct conv, if the kernel is memory bound, we need to maximize the output/input ratio. Computing more output channels per block helps because: - Same input tile read, more weights, more outputs computed - But with C_out=64, we can at most have 64 output channels What about computing all 64 output channels for a smaller spatial tile? - Spatial tile: 8x8 = 64 positions - Block has 256 threads, each computes 64/256 = 0.25... doesn't divide cleanly. Total FMAs per block: 128 * 64 * 1,440 = 11,796,480. - Total memory read: input 36KB + weights for 64 channels = 64*32*5*9*4 = 368,640 bytes = 369KB. Total ~405KB. - AI = 11.8M / 405K = 29 FMAs/byte. At 3.35 TB/s, this gives ~97 FP32 TFLOPS. H100 has 67 TFLOPS, so we're now COMPUTE BOUND! This is great. The bottleneck shifts to compute, and if we can hit 67 TFLOPS (or a reasonable fraction), we'll beat PyTorch. So the plan: - Block computes a spatial tile of 8x16 (128 positions) for ALL 64 output channels. - 256 threads, each computes output for (128 * 64) / 256 = 32 output elements. - We can assign: each thread handles 32 spatial positions for 1 output channel, or some combination. Actually, 128 spatial positions and 64 channels -> 8,192 total output values. With 256 threads, each thread handles 32 values. So each thread handles 16 values. Actually, we can also assign: - 128 threads handle spatial positions (each computes one position for two channels? Very small! Wait, 12*16*32 = 6,144. At 4 bytes per float: 24,576 bytes. That's tiny! This means we can potentially fit many blocks per SM. In fact, we could use a larger spatial tile. What about 16x16 spatial positions with all 64 channels? - Input: (16+4)*(16+8)*32 = 20*24*32 = 15,360 floats = 61,440 bytes. Still well within limits. - Outputs: 256 * 64 = 16,384 - 256 threads -> 64 outputs per thread - Each warp handles 8 channels for 256 positions = 2,048 outputs - Warp has 32 threads, so each thread handles 64 positions for 8 channels? 64 * 8 = 512 outputs per thread. That's a lot of compute! Actually, 16,384 outputs / 256 threads = 64 outputs per thread. So each thread handles 64 outputs. If each warp handles 8 channels for all 256 positions: 8 * 256 = 2,048 outputs per warp. Wait, 2,048 outputs per warp * 8 warps = 16,384. And 2,048 / 32 threads = 64 outputs per thread. Yes! So each thread computes 64 outputs. This could be: - 64 positions for 1 channel - 32 positions for 2 channels - 16 positions for 4 channels - 8 positions for 8 channels - etc. If a warp handles 8 channels for all 256 positions, and there are 32 threads: - Each thread handles 256/32 = 8 positions for 8 channels = 64 outputs. Great! So each thread computes 8 spatial positions * 8 output channels. Wait, but warp collaboration on shared memory would be: all 32 threads in a warp need different input elements (different spatial positions). They access s_input[c][h_i][w_i] where h_i, w_i differ by thread. If stored as [c][h][w], this is still a bank conflict issue. But we can store shared memory as [H][W][C] instead! Let me reconsider: - `__shared__ float s_input[20][24][32]` (padded dimensions for 16x16 tile with 5x9 kernel) - Each thread accesses `s_input[thread_h][thread_w][c]` for its spatial position. - For a fixed c, threads in a warp access different h, w. With layout [h][w][c], consecutive h rows are separated by 24*32=768 floats. 768*4 = 3072 bytes. Bank index = (offset / 4) % 32. 768 / 4 = 192. 192 % 32 = 0. So all threads accessing the same w but different h land on the SAME bank! This is terrible for bank conflicts. If layout is [c][h][w]: consecutive c are separated by 20*24=480 floats. 480 floats = 1920 bytes. 480 % 32 = 16. So thread 0 accesses bank 16, thread 1 accesses bank 16+1=17, etc. If threads in warp have consecutive c, they have consecutive banks with a stride of 1 - no bank conflicts! BUT: in our kernel, for a given (kh, kw), all threads want the same c but different h, w. With [c][h][w] layout, for fixed c, consecutive h values differ by 24 floats (96 bytes). Bank index = base + h*24 + w. For threads accessing same w but different h: banks differ by 24 mod 32 = 24. So bank indices are 0, 24, 16, 8, 0, 24, ... for h=0,1,2,3,4,5... For a warp of 32 threads where each has a unique (h, w) pair in a 16x16 grid, the bank access pattern is complex. Actually, let me reconsider. A warp has 32 threads. If block is 16x16, the warp consists of 32 consecutive threads in threadIdx. Thread index = threadIdx.y * 16 + threadIdx.x. So warp 0 has: - Threads 0-15: threadIdx.y=0, threadIdx.x=0..15 - Threads 16-31: threadIdx.y=1, threadIdx.x=0..15 Wait, actually in CUDA, threads in a warp are consecutive in threadIdx.x first (since x is fastest varying). In a 2D block (16, 16): - Thread (0,0) = 0, (0,1) = 1, ..., (0,15) = 15 - Thread (1,0) = 16, (1,1) = 17, ..., (1,15) = 31 So first 16 threads of warp 0 have y=0, x=0..15. Next 16 have y=1, x=0..15. Under [c][h][w] layout, all threads access s_input[c][threadIdx.y + kh][threadIdx.x + kw]. For first 16 threads (y=0, x=0..15): offsets = c*480 + kh*24 + kw + x. Banks for consecutive x: (base + x) % 32. So x=0->bank b, x=1->bank b+1, ..., x=15->bank b+15. No conflicts. For next 16 threads (y=1, x=0..15): offsets = c*480 + (1+kh)*24 + kw + x = c*480 + kh*24 + 24 + kw + x. Banks = (base + 24 + x) % 32 = (b + 24 + x) % 32. First half: banks b to b+15. Second half: banks b+24 to b+39 mod 32 = b+24 to b+7 (wrapping). Overlapping banks: b+24 to b+15 (wrapping). This is b, b+1, ..., b+15 for first half and b+24, b+25, ..., b+31, 0, 1, ..., b+7 for second half. Overlap: banks 0 to b+7 AND banks b to b+15. The union of overlap is from max(0, b) to min(b+7, b+15) if b+7 >= b... actually since b+7 < b+15 always, the overlap is from b to b+7 (8 banks). So 8 banks are accessed by two threads each. 2-way bank conflicts for those 8 banks. To reduce this, we can use a larger block in x dimension. For example, if TILE_W = 32: - Block = (32, 8) -> 256 threads - Warp 0: threads 0-31 all have y=0, x=0..31 - First half-warp (16 threads): y=0, x=0..15. Banks: b to b+15. - Second half-warp: y=0, x=16..31. Banks: b+16 to b+31. No overlap! This is much better! But our output width is 504, which is divisible by many numbers. 504/32 = 15.75. So we can use TILE_W = 32, TILE_H = 8 (since 508/8 = 63.5). Actually, 504 is divisible by 8 (63), 9 (56), 12 (42), 14 (36), 16 (31.5 no), 18 (28), 21 (24), 24 (21), 28 (18). 508 is divisible by 4 (127). So for H_out=508 and W_out=504: - We can use TILE_H = 4, TILE_W = 32 -> 4*32=128 spatial positions per block - Or TILE_H = 8, TILE_W = 16 -> 128 positions (but 16 causes 2-way conflict... but with [c][h][w] and padding to 25 maybe it's better) - Or TILE_H = 4, TILE_W = 32 -> no conflict Let's use TILE_H = 4, TILE_W = 32. Block size = (32, 8) = 256 threads. But we only have 4*32 = 128 spatial positions, and 256 threads. We could have each thread handle 2 spatial positions, or compute multiple channels. Actually, let me pair threads: since each thread computes multiple channels anyway, we can have each thread compute for one spatial position but multiple channels. Wait, what if we use TILE_H=8, TILE_W=16, block=(16, 16)=256 threads. Warp layout: 16 threads per row. First warp: rows 0,1 with 16 threads each. With [c][h][w] layout: - Row 0, x=0..15: banks b to b+15 - Row 1, x=0..15: banks (b+24) to (b+39 mod 32) = b+24 to b+7 - Overlap: b to b+7 (8 banks, 2-way conflict) 16-way broadcast on 8 banks + no conflict on 24 banks. Average conflict degree = (8*2 + 24*1) / 32 = 40/32 = 1.25. Not too bad. What if we pad the width to be odd? [c][h][PADDED_W] where PADDED_W = 25. Offsets for row 0: b + x Row 1: b + 25 + x = b + x + 25 mod 32 First half: b to b+15 mod 32 Second half: b+25 to b+40 mod 32 = b+25 to b+8 Overlap: b+8 to b+15 (8 banks). What if we store as [W][H][C]? Row 0, x=0..15: w varies, h fixed. So memory is non-consecutive for threads in warp. Access pattern is strided by H. If H=20, consecutive w differ by 20 floats. 20 % 32 = 20. Bank access: b, b+20, b+8, b+28, b+16, b+4, b+24, b+12, b, ... This is complex but might have fewer conflicts. Let me calculate for [w][h][c] with W=24, H=20: Thread (x, y) accesses offset = x * (H*C) + y * C + c. For first half (y=0): offset = x * 640 + c. Banks = (x * 640 / 4 + c/4) % 32 = (x * 160 + c/4) % 32. 160 mod 32 = 0. So banks = c/4 % 32 for all x in half-warp. ALL threads in first half access the SAME bank! TOTAL CONFLICT! So [c][h][w] is definitely better. Given that the bank conflict is manageable (1.25x for 2 rows), let me stick with [c][h][w] layout and TILE_H=8, TILE_W=16. Actually, let me try something else: what if I operate in a way where each warp accesses a contiguous chunk in memory? What if each thread computes multiple channels for one spatial position, and threads in a warp have the SAME spatial position but different channels? Then they all access the SAME memory location in s_input. This is a broadcast - zero bank conflicts! YES! This is the optimal pattern for shared memory! Let's redesign: - Block computes a tile of spatial positions - But each WARP computes the same spatial position(s) for different output channels - Wait, we need each warp to have all threads at the same spatial position. Then what do they compute? Different output channels! If positions are adjacent, we can share input through shared memory. But if each warp handles a DIFFERENT position, we need all their inputs in shared memory at once, which explodes shared memory usage. Alternative: process 1 spatial position at a time per block, with ALL 64 channels. - 1 position * 64 channels = 64 outputs - 8 warps, each computes 8 channels for the same position (wait, 8 warps * 8 channels = 64 channels!) - 32 threads per warp. The inner loop is over c_in, kh, kw. ```cpp for (int pos = 0; pos < 4; ++pos) { int h = compute_h(lane_id, pos); int w = compute_w(lane_id, pos); for (int oc = 0; oc < 8; ++oc) { float sum = 0; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { float in_val = s_input[c][h + kh][w + kw]; float w_val = weight[...]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } output[...] = sum; } } ``` For a fixed (c, kh, kw), lanes 0..15 access s_input[c][0 + kh][0 + kw] to s_input[c][0 + kh][15 + kw]. These are 16 consecutive floats in the w dimension. With [c][h][w] layout, they are consecutive in memory. 16 consecutive floats = 64 bytes. Does this cause bank conflicts? No! Consecutive memory accesses are spread across 16 banks (64 bytes / 4 bytes per bank spacing). Actually, shared memory has 32 banks, each 4 bytes. 16 consecutive floats occupy banks 0-15 perfectly, no conflicts. Lanes 16..31 access s_input[c][1 + kh][0 + kw] to s_input[c][1 + kh][15 + kw]. These are also 16 consecutive floats but at a different h row. The starting offset differs by 24 floats (one h row). 24 floats = 96 bytes. Starting bank = (base + 24) % 32. If base=0, lane 16 accesses bank 24. Banks 24-31, then 0-7. First half: banks 0-15. Second half: banks 24-31, 0-7. Overlap: banks 0-7. 2-way bank conflicts for 8 banks. But wait! We can avoid this by processing one row at a time within a warp. What if we assign the warp to handle only even h rows or only odd h rows? Then we need more warps. For a fixed (c, kh, kw): - Row 0: banks b to b+15 - Row 2: banks (b + 2*24) to (b + 2*24 + 15) = b+16 to b+31. No overlap! Because 24*2 = 48. 48 mod 32 = 16. First row: banks b to b+15. Second row: banks b+16 to b+31. NO OVERLAP! If we structure the kernel so that adjacent threads (in a warp) read adjacent memory, we'll get good global memory coalescing and high L2 hit rates. Direct approach without shared memory: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_cache_kernel(...) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total_outputs) return; int out_w = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int out_h = tmp % H_out; tmp /= H_out; int out_c = tmp % C_out; int out_b = tmp / C_out; float sum = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { float* in_ptr = input + ((out_b * C_in + c) * H + out_h) * W + out_w; float* w_ptr = weight + ((out_c * C_in + c) * KH) * KW; for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { sum += in_ptr[kh * W + kw] * w_ptr[kh * KW + kw]; } } } output[idx] = sum; } ``` But this has the issue that threads in a warp compute different (out_c, out_b, out_h, out_w). If they all have different out_c, they access different weights, causing weight cache misses. But the input accesses might still coalesce if out_w and out_h are the same for threads in the warp. The problem is the linear mapping doesn't guarantee that. Each thread computes all 64 output channels for its position: ```cpp int h_out = blockIdx.y * 4 + threadIdx.y; // blockIdx.y for H tiles int w_out = blockIdx.x * 16 + threadIdx.x; // blockIdx.x for W tiles int b = blockIdx.z; for (int c_out = 0; c_out < 64; ++c_out) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < 32; ++c_in) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { float in = input[((b * 32 + c_in) * 512 + (h_out + kh)) * 512 + (w_out + kw)]; float w = weight[((c_out * 32 + c_in) * 5 + kh) * 9 + kw]; sum += in * w; } } } output[((b * 64 + c_out) * 508 + h_out) * 504 + w_out] = sum; } ``` Let's analyze the memory access: - For a fixed (c_out, c_in, kh, kw), all 64 threads in the block access: - Input: thread (tx, ty) accesses h_out = blockH*4 + ty, w_out = blockW*16 + tx. These are 16 consecutive floats -> good coalescing! The next warp (tx=0..15, ty=1) accesses a different row, also 16 consecutive floats -> also good coalescing. - Weights: for fixed (c_out, c_in, kh, kw), all threads access the SAME weight value. This is a broadcast! Very efficient. This kernel actually has really good memory access patterns! Let me write a cleaner version of the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_direct_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int KH, int KW, int H_out, int W_out ) { const int TILE_H = 4; const int TILE_W = 16; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int w_tile = blockIdx.x; int h_tile = blockIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; int w_out = w_tile * TILE_W + tx; int h_out = h_tile * TILE_H + ty; // Check if within bounds if (h_out >= H_out || w_out >= W_out) return; // Compute base indices int in_base_stride_h = W; int in_base = ((b * C_in) * H + h_out) * W + w_out; int out_base = ((b * C_out) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out; float accum[64]; // Can we hold 64 floats in registers? 64 * 4 = 256 bytes per thread. With 64 threads = 16KB of registers for accum alone. // That's a lot. 256 bytes per thread * 64 threads = 16KB accumulators. // Plus weight loads, loop variables, etc. Total per thread registers might be ~300 bytes. At 64 registers per thread (out of 255 on H100), this should be fine. #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) accum[oc] = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { int in_c_base = in_base + c * H * W; int w_c_base = c * KH * KW; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { // Load input row int in_row_offset = in_c_base + kh * W; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { float in_val = input[in_row_offset + kw]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { float w_val = weight[(oc * C_in + c) * KH * KW + kh * KW + kw]; accum[oc] += in_val * w_val; } } } } #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { output[out_base + oc * H_out * W_out] = accum[oc]; } } ``` Wait, this has issues: 1. `int w_c_base = c * KH * KW;` should be `int w_c_base = c * KH * KW;` but weight indexing is `(oc * C_in + c) * KH * KW + kh * KW + kw`. So for fixed c and kh, kw, all oc use different memory addresses for weights. In the inner loop over oc, we read strided weight values. These are not consecutive and not broadcast. To make weight access a broadcast, we need each thread to compute a different oc while fixing spatial. But with 64 threads all computing the same spatial position, each thread can compute one oc. Then for a given (c, kh, kw), all threads access: - input: same in_val (broadcast) - weight: weight[(oc * C_in + c) * KH * KW + kh * KW + kw] where oc = threadIdx. This means threads in a warp access weight[0], weight[1], weight[2], ... for consecutive oc. Are these consecutive in memory? weight[(0 * 32 + c) * 45 + offset] = weight[c*45 + offset] weight[(1 * 32 + c) * 45 + offset] = weight[32*45 + c*45 + offset] = weight[(32+c)*45 + offset] These are spaced by 45 floats (180 bytes) apart. For 32 consecutive threads: offsets are c*45+offset, (32+c)*45+offset, (64+c)*45+offset, etc. Wait, oc goes from 0 to 63. If threads in a warp take oc = 0..31 and oc = 32..63, then: - Thread 0 accesses weight[c*45 + offset] - Thread 1 accesses weight[45 + c*45 + offset] = weight[(c+1)*45 + offset] - These are 45 floats apart, not consecutive. 45*4 = 180 bytes. Bank/index: 45 % 32 = 13. So bank access pattern for weight is strided by 13. This is not ideal for L1/L2 cache and definitely not a broadcast. But if we assign 64 threads where each handles one oc for the spatial position, then all 64 output channels are computed. This uses 64 threads per block and requires iterating over many spatial positions with many blocks. No, we want one thread per spatial position for good output locality. Let's try: - Block: 64 threads (8x8) for 8x8 spatial tile - Each thread computes ALL 64 output channels for 1 spatial position - Total block outputs: 64 pos * 64 ch = 4,096 - Each of 64 threads computes 64 outputs For a given (c_in, kh, kw): - All 64 threads need the same weight values for their respective c_out. Thread oc needs weight for c_out=oc. These are strided. - But the 64 threads need different input values (different positions). These are fairly coalesced. To optimize weight access, we can pre-load the current (c_in, kh, kw) weights for all c_out into shared memory: ```cpp __shared__ float s_weight[C_out]; // After loading input values... // Cooperative load of weights for this (c_in, kh, kw): for (int i = threadIdx; i < C_out; i += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { s_weight[i] = weight[...]; } __syncthreads(); ``` Then each thread accesses `s_weight[oc]` which is fast shared memory. 64 floats = 256 bytes. Very small! Yes! This is excellent. For each (c_in, kh, kw), we load 64 weights into shared memory (very fast), and then all threads access them without bank conflicts (each thread accesses a different bank since 64 floats span 64 banks and we have 64 threads; actually 32 threads in a warp access 32 different elements which are in 32 different banks -> no conflict!) Wait, but if threads in a warp access s_weight[oc] for oc=0..31, and s_weight is just 64 floats, then thread 0 accesses bank 0, thread 1 accesses bank 1, ..., thread 31 accesses bank 31 (since each float is 4 bytes and there are 32 banks, each spaced by 4 bytes). NO BANK CONFLICTS! This is perfect! And for the next warp (oc=32..63): same, no conflicts. So the refined kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_direct_kernel(...) { const int TILE_H = 8; const int TILE_W = 8; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int w_tile = blockIdx.x; int h_tile = blockIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; int w_out = w_tile * TILE_W + tx; int h_out = h_tile * TILE_H + ty; if (h_out >= H_out || w_out >= W_out) return; __shared__ float s_weight[C_out]; float accum[C_out]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) accum[oc] = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { // Load weight for all output channels if (tx == 0 && ty == 0) { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { s_weight[oc] = weight[((oc * C_in + c) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; } } __syncthreads(); float in_val = input[((b * C_in + c) * H + (h_out + kh)) * W + (w_out + kw)]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { accum[oc] += in_val * s_weight[oc]; } __syncthreads(); } } } #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { output[((b * C_out + oc) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = accum[oc]; } } ``` Wait, using if (tx==0 && ty==0) to load all weights creates serialization. With C_out=64, that's 64 loads by one thread. For all (c, kh, kw) = 32*5*9 = 1440 iterations, that's 1440 * 64 = 92,160 loads total by one thread. This would be very slow. Better to have all threads load weights cooperatively: ```cpp for (int oc = tx + ty * blockDim.x; oc < C_out; oc += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { s_weight[oc] = weight[...]; } ``` With 64 threads, each loads 1 weight. This is parallel and efficient. Revised loading: ```cpp int tid = ty * blockDim.x + tx; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { // Load weight cooperatively for (int oc = tid; oc < C_out; oc += 64) { s_weight[oc] = weight[((oc * C_in + c) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; } __syncthreads(); float in_val = ...; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { accum[oc] += in_val * s_weight[oc]; } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, but the `oc` loop over 64 with `#pragma unroll` will unroll to 64 iterations. With `accum[64]` as an array and `s_weight[64]` as shared memory, each iteration is a couple of instructions (load shared, FMA, store register). Unrolling 64 iterations is fine. One potential issue with our kernel: the writes are not fully coalesced in the outer oc loop. We write to `out_base + oc * H_out * W_out` for oc=0..63. Each write is separated by 508*504 floats. When the warp writes oc=0, all threads write to addresses in channel 0. When they write oc=1, all threads write to addresses in channel 1, which is ~1MB away. This means each write goes to a completely different cache line / memory segment. The total output per thread is 64 floats written sparsely across memory. 64 writes per thread * 256 threads = 16,384 writes. The total output for a block is 16 * 16 * 64 = 16,384 floats = 64KB. Actually, if we transpose the output layout, we could coalesce writes differently. But PyTorch expects NCHW format. A potential optimization: instead of having each thread write 64 scattered floats, have the threads cooperate to transpose and write in a more coalesced manner. But this is complex. Given that total memory write is only 64KB per block, and memory bandwidth is 3.35 TB/s, writing 64KB takes ~0.02 microseconds. So write time is negligible compared to compute time (~0.7 microseconds). Let me also consider whether we should use float4 or other vectorization for input reads. Actually, I can store accumulators in an array and loop with `#pragma unroll`. The CUDA compiler handles this quite well in practice for arrays that fit in registers. Let me write the full kernel code: ```cuda __global__ void __launch_bounds__(96, 12) // 96 threads, willing to have up to 12 blocks per SM (but limited by registers) conv2d_direct_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int KH, int KW, int H_out, int W_out ) { const int TILE_H = 4; const int TILE_W = 24; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int w_tile = blockIdx.x; int h_tile = blockIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; int w_out = w_tile * TILE_W + tx; int h_out = h_tile * TILE_H + ty; if (h_out >= H_out || w_out >= W_out) return; __shared__ float s_w[C_out]; float accum[C_out]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { accum[oc] = 0.0f; } int tid = ty * TILE_W + tx; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { // Precompute input base for this channel and spatial position int in_base = ((b * C_in + c) * H + h_out) * W + w_out; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { // Load weight for all output channels cooperatively for (int oc = tid; oc < C_out; oc += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { s_w[oc] = weight[((oc * C_in + c) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; } __syncthreads(); float in_val = input[in_base + kh * W + kw]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { accum[oc] += in_val * s_w[oc]; } __syncthreads(); } } } int out_base = ((b * C_out) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { output[out_base + oc * H_out * W_out] = accum[oc]; } } ``` Wait, there might be an issue with `__syncthreads()` inside `#pragma unroll` loops. If the loop is unrolled, there would be multiple `__syncthreads()` calls in a row. This is actually fine as long as the control flow is convergent (all threads in the block reach every `__syncthreads()`). Since there's no divergence inside the unrolled loops, this should be fine. But `__syncthreads()` in an unrolled loop could be expensive because each unrolled instance is a separate barrier. With 5*9=45 barriers per channel * 32 channels = 1,440 barriers per block. Each barrier has some overhead. This might be significant! Actually, `__syncthreads()` on H100 is relatively fast (~10-20 cycles), but 1,440 of them = ~14,000-28,000 cycles per block. With 2 GHz, that's 7-14 microseconds. That might dominate the runtime. Can we avoid `__syncthreads()`? We need it to ensure all threads have loaded `s_w` before any thread reads it. But we can restructure: Instead of loading weights for each (kw) into shared memory, we could load them directly into registers for each thread. But different threads need different weights. Wait, if we know that all threads in a warp execute in lockstep (within a warp), then do we need `__syncthreads()`? For shared memory writes by threads in the same warp, the writes are guaranteed to be visible to reads in subsequent instructions within the same warp? Actually, the CUDA memory model requires `__syncwarp()` or `__syncthreads()` for intra-warp visibility in some cases, but on modern GPUs, writes from a thread are visible to other threads in the same warp without explicit synchronization if we use the right memory ordering. But we also have threads in different warps loading into shared memory. What if we assign weight loading to specific warps? Or what if we load weights into registers instead? Alternative: each thread loads the weights it needs directly into registers. Each thread needs 64 weights per (c, kh, kw). That's 64 floats. But they are the same 64 weights for all threads! We'd be reading 64 floats from global memory 96 times = 9,216 redundant reads. With 21,336 blocks, that's a LOT of redundant reads. Shared memory is clearly better for weight sharing. But the barrier overhead is the issue. Can we reduce barrier count? What if we load weights for all kw at once? For fixed (c, kh), we need weights for all 9 kw values and all 64 channels. That's 576 floats = 2,304 bytes. We could load all of them into shared memory at once, then process all 9 kw values without additional barriers. ```cpp float in_vals[9]; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { in_vals[kw] = input[in_base + kh * W + kw]; } // Load all weights for this (c, kh) __shared__ float s_w[9][64]; for (int idx = tid; idx < 9 * 64; idx += 96) { int kw = idx / 64; int oc = idx % 64; s_w[kw][oc] = weight[((oc * 32 + c) * 5 + kh) * 9 + kw]; } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { float in_val = in_vals[kw]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) { accum[oc] += in_val * s_w[kw][oc]; } } __syncthreads(); // before next load ``` This reduces barriers from 45 to 5 per channel (one per kh). That's 5 * 32 = 160 barriers per block. Much better! And `s_w[9][64]` = 576 floats = 2,304 bytes. Very small. Let me check bank conflicts for `s_w[kw][oc]`. If threads in a warp access `s_w[kw][oc]` where oc varies by thread, and the array is [9][64]: - Thread i accesses `s_w[kw][i]`. Address = kw*64 + i. For a warp, if all threads have same kw but different i (0..31), addresses are consecutive. No bank conflicts! - If warp has different kw values, then addresses are spaced by 64. 64%32=0. So consecutive threads with same i but different kw would access the same bank. But in our kernel, all threads in a warp process the same kw at the same time (in the outer kw loop). So no conflict. This is much better. Let's use this. Similarly, we could load weights for all 5 kh values at once: `s_w[5][9][64]` = 2,880 floats = 11,520 bytes. Still small. Then only 1 barrier per channel! 32 barriers total per block. Let's see if we can fit and if it's worth it. ```cpp __shared__ float s_w[5][9][64]; for (int idx = tid; idx < 5 * 9 * 64; idx += 96) { int oc = idx % 64; int kw = (idx / 64) % 9; int kh = idx / (64 * 9); s_w[kh][kw][oc] = weight[((oc * 32 + c) * 5 + kh) * 9 + kw]; } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { float in_val = input[in_base + kh * W + kw]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) { accum[oc] += in_val * s_w[kh][kw][oc]; } } } __syncthreads(); // for next channel ``` This is excellent! 32 barriers per block total. Wait, but loading 2,880 floats into shared memory for each channel. 96 threads each load 30 floats (2,880 / 96 = 30). That's 30 loads per thread, well-coalesced because the weight layout matches the loop. Let me verify weight layout: weight shape: [C_out=64, C_in=32, KH=5, KW=9] In PyTorch, this is contiguous in memory with stride [32*5*9, 5*9, 9, 1] = [1440, 45, 9, 1]. For fixed c, as we iterate kh and kw with fixed oc: `weight[oc * 1440 + c * 45 + kh * 9 + kw]` Consecutive kh and kw give consecutive memory (within a 45-float chunk for the c slice). But we're loading with `idx` from 0 to 2,879: - idx=0: oc=0, kw=0, kh=0 -> weight[0*1440 + c*45 + 0*9 + 0] = weight[c*45] - idx=1: oc=1, kw=0, kh=0 -> weight[1*1440 + c*45 + 0*9 + 0] = weight[1440 + c*45] These are 1,440 floats apart. This is TERRIBLE coalescing! Oh no! The weight layout is [C_out][C_in][KH][KW]. For fixed c, varying oc means accessing memory at stride 1,440 floats. This is the complete opposite of coalesced. Can we instead load by fixing oc and varying kh, kw? But we need all oc values in shared memory. If we load: - Thread 0 loads oc=0, all kh, all kw - Thread 1 loads oc=1, all kh, all kw Each thread loads 45 floats that are consecutive in memory. 45 floats is not a multiple of cache line size (128 bytes = 32 floats), but close. Each thread does 2 loads (maybe float4 + float1). This is much better! Let's restructure the load: ```cpp for (int oc = ty; oc < 64; oc += 4) { // each thread handles some oc based on ty (there are 4 rows) // Load all weights for this oc and current c_in const float* w_ptr = weight + (oc * 32 + c) * 45; float* s_ptr = &s_w[0][0][oc]; // pointer to s_w[0][0][oc] // But s_w is [5][9][64]. We need to store in a pattern that allows broadcast or coalesced access. } ``` Wait, `s_w[kh][kw][oc]` has `oc` as the fastest varying dimension. If we store this way, then threads in a warp accessing the same kh, kw but different oc will hit different banks (good). But loading into this layout with each thread handling one oc means each thread writes 45 floats but strided by 64 in shared memory. `s_w[kh][kw][oc]` address = (kh * 9 + kw) * 64 + oc. Thread for oc=0: writes to addresses 0, 64, 128, 192, ... (all in bank 0). Thread for oc=1: bank 1, etc. This causes bank conflicts during loading! 32 threads each writing to their own bank... actually no, each thread writes 45 floats across 45 rows (kh, kw combinations), all in the same bank. Since each thread writes to a different bank (for different oc), and consecutive threads handle consecutive oc, we get one thread per bank -> NO bank conflicts for writes! But each thread does 45 independent writes to shared memory. This is fine. Actually, let me re-examine. We have `s_w[kh][kw][oc]` with dimensions [5][9][64]. The total is 2,880 floats. We can organize the load by assigning each thread one `oc` value. Then each thread loads 45 floats from global memory (consecutive, good coalescing for global loads) and writes them to shared memory at locations: `&s_w[0][0][oc]`, `&s_w[0][1][oc]`, ..., `&s_w[4][8][oc]`. In shared memory, these are strided by 64 floats. 64 * 4 = 256 bytes. Bank index = (offset / 4) % 32. 256 / 4 = 64. 64 % 32 = 0. So each write to the next kw is in the same bank! Within one thread, all 45 writes go to the same bank. That's fine because it's the same thread, not different threads in a warp. But for the compute phase, threads in a warp access `s_w[kh][kw][thread_oc]` for the same kh, kw. Consecutive threads have consecutive thread_oc. In [kh][kw][oc] layout, consecutive oc are consecutive in memory. So a warp reads 32 consecutive floats = 128 bytes = 4 cache lines. No bank conflicts. This is perfect! But we need to load 64 oc's. With 96 threads, we could assign: threads 0..63 load oc=0..63. The remaining 32 threads help or are idle. Actually, 96 threads > 64, so threads 64..95 are idle during weight loading. That's fine. Wait, but only 32 threads in a warp execute in lockstep. If we have 96 threads in 3 warps, warp 0 loads oc 0..31, warp 1 loads oc 32..63, warp 2 is idle. This uses 2 warps. But within each warp, threads load consecutive oc's. Addresses in global memory are `weight + (oc * 32 + c) * 45`. These are spaced by 1440 floats = 5,760 bytes. Consecutive threads access memory 5,760 bytes apart. This is NOT coalesced! Oh no, global memory coalescing is bad for weight loads if each thread loads one oc. Coalescing requires threads in a warp to access consecutive addresses. But here, thread i accesses address `base + i * 1440`. That's a stride of 1440 floats. On H100, this would generate 32 separate memory transactions per warp. Very bad. To fix this, we have two options: 1. Have all threads load the SAME oc (broadcast/broadcast), then move to next oc. This means 64 iterations of loading one float per thread... no, each thread would load one float for a specific (kh, kw), and all threads load the same oc but different (kh, kw). 2. Transpose the weight matrix on the CPU or in the constructor so that oc is the fastest varying dimension. Actually, in PyTorch, the weight is [C_out, C_in, KH, KW]. We can't easily transpose it without changing the semantics. But we COULD transpose the weight in the constructor and adjust the indexing. What if in `ModelNew.__init__`, after calling `super().__init__()`, we transpose the weight to [C_in, KH, KW, C_out]? Then in the kernel, for fixed c, kh, kw, weights for different oc are consecutive in memory! This would GREATLY improve weight loading. Yes! We can do this: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, ...): super().__init__() self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d(...) # Transpose weight to [C_in, KH, KW, C_out] for better memory layout with torch.no_grad(): self.conv2d.weight.data = self.conv2d.weight.data.permute(1, 2, 3, 0).contiguous() ``` Then in the kernel, weight indexing for fixed c, kh, kw and varying oc becomes: `weight + c * (KH * KW * C_out) + kh * (KW * C_out) + kw * C_out + oc` For varying oc, these are consecutive! Excellent! For loading weights: ```cpp // s_w[kh][kw][oc] where kh, kw index and oc is fastest varying // In the transposed weight, for fixed c, the data is laid out as: // [kh=0][kw=0][oc=0..63], [kh=0][kw=1][oc=0..63], ..., [kh=4][kw=8][oc=0..63] // So loading 64 consecutive floats for one (kh, kw) is trivial // Each thread loads one oc... no, better: have all 96 threads load one (kh, kw) block at a time // For one (kh, kw), there are 64 floats (oc=0..63). 96 threads can load them all in one go // with 64 threads loading 1 float each (coalesced!) and 32 threads idle. // Or we load 64 floats as 16 float4s using 16 threads ``` Wait, if we load 64 floats for one (kh, kw): - Address in transposed weight: `w_ptr = weight + c * 5 * 9 * 64 + kh * 9 * 64 + kw * 64`. - 16 threads can each load a float4: `((float4*)w_ptr)[threadIdx.x]` for threadIdx.x=0..15. - This loads 64 floats in 16 coalesced transactions (each float4 is 128-bit, coalesced across 16 threads). - Then we write to shared memory. If `s_w[kh][kw][oc]` is stored with oc as fastest varying, a float4 write by thread i writes to `&s_w[kh][kw][i*4]`. - Shared memory banks: 128 bits = 4 floats. A float4 write by one thread touches 4 consecutive banks. 16 threads writing float4s touch 64 consecutive banks ~ 0-63. But there are only 32 banks of 4 bytes each. Wait, float4 is 16 bytes. On H100 with 4-byte banks, a float4 spans 4 banks. 16 threads * 4 banks = 64 bank accesses. But 64 mod 32 = 0, so banks 0-31 are each accessed twice? Let me think: Thread 0: banks 0,1,2,3 Thread 1: banks 4,5,6,7 ... Thread 7: banks 28,29,30,31 Thread 8: banks 32,33,34,35 -> 0,1,2,3 Thread 9: banks 4,5,6,7 ... Thread 15: banks 28,29,30,31 Oh no, threads 0 and 8 both access banks 0-3. 2-way bank conflict! With float4 writes, to avoid bank conflicts, we need either: 1. Only 8 threads writing float4s (covering 32 banks each once), or 2. Use a 32-bit interleaved (swizzled) storage pattern. Alternatively, use 32 threads, each writing 2 floats. Then thread i writes banks i*2 and i*2+1. 32 threads cover all 64 banks (2 per thread), and banks don't overlap since each bank is handled by one thread. NO BANK CONFLICTS! So: 32 threads each load 2 floats from global memory and write to shared memory. - Global loads: 32 threads * 2 floats = 64 floats. Threads 0-31 load addresses w_ptr+0, w_ptr+2, w_ptr+4, ..., w_ptr+62. - Are these coalesced? Yes! Consecutive threads load consecutive floats. Two 32-bit loads per thread. The hardware might merge them. - Actually, on recent GPUs, loading 2 consecutive floats per thread is also coalesced (64 bytes per warp per load, which is 1-2 cache lines). Or, we can have 32 threads each write to shared memory directly. But we want 96 threads total in the block. We can assign a warp (32 threads) to do the loading, and the rest wait. Actually, a simpler approach: use `__shared__ float s_w[5][9][64];`. In the load loop: ```cpp if (threadIdx.x < 16) { float4 w4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(w_ptr)[threadIdx.x]; reinterpret_cast<float4*>(&s_w[kh][kw][0])[threadIdx.x] = w4; } ``` With 16 threads and float4: Thread 0 writes to s_w[kh][kw][0..3], touching banks 0,1,2,3. Thread 1 writes to s_w[kh][kw][4..7], touching banks 4,5,6,7. ... Thread 7 writes to s_w[kh][kw][28..31], touching banks 28,29,30,31. Thread 8 writes to s_w[kh][kw][32..35], touching banks 0,1,2,3 (since 32 mod 32 = 0). Conflict with thread 0! So with 16 threads, threads 0 and 8 conflict. With 8 threads, no conflict! But 8 threads loading 8 float4s = 32 floats. We need 64 floats. What about 32 threads each loading 1 float2? Thread i loads float2 to s_w[kh][kw][i*2..i*2+1]. Banks: i*2 and i*2+1. Thread i and thread i+16: (i+16)*2 = i*2 + 32. 32 mod 32 = 0. So thread i writes banks i*2, i*2+1. Thread i+16 writes banks i*2, i*2+1 (since (i+16)*2 = 2i+32 ≡ 2i mod 32). CONFLICT! What about 64 threads each loading 1 float? Thread i writes to bank i. Thread i+32 writes to bank i+32 ≡ i. CONFLICT! So if we have more than 32 threads in a warp writing to the shared memory array, we get conflicts because there are only 32 banks and our array is aligned to 32. To avoid this, we can either: 1. Use only 32 threads for loading. 64 floats / 32 threads = 2 floats per thread. Each thread does 2 separate float loads/writes. Banks: thread i uses banks 2i and 2i+1. For i=0..31, these are 0..63 ≡ 0..31 each used once. NO CONFLICT! But we need to solve the ordering so that the 2 floats a thread loads are consecutive in global memory. And we only use 32 threads out of 96. 2. Pad the shared memory to have odd dimensions or use a swizzled layout. For example, `__shared__ float s_w[5][9][66];`. Then thread i accesses bank `i*66/4 % 32` for float4... this gets complicated. Actually, for simplicity, let me just use separate float loads by 32 threads: ```cpp if (tid < 32) { s_w[kh][kw][tid * 2] = w_ptr[tid * 2]; s_w[kh][kw][tid * 2 + 1] = w_ptr[tid * 2 + 1]; } else if (tid < 64) { // But we only have 96 threads and need all 64 loaded... // Actually, 32 threads can handle all 64 floats by loading 2 each. } ``` Wait, `tid` ranges from 0 to 95. We can do: ```cpp for (int i = tid; i < 64; i += 96) { s_w[kh][kw][i] = w_ptr[i]; } ``` For a warp with tid=0..31: - Threads 0-31 each load one float. w_ptr[i] is consecutive, so global load is coalesced. - Writes to s_w[kh][kw][i] for i=0..31. Banks 0..31. No conflicts. For the next warp (tid=32..63): loads w_ptr[32..63]. Writes to banks 32..63 ≡ 0..31. But this is in a DIFFERENT warp. Warp 0 and warp 1 don't cause bank conflicts because they execute independently. Bank conflicts only happen within a warp. For the third warp (tid=64..95): loads w_ptr[64..95] - wait, there are only 64 elements, so i=64..95 don't execute (i >= 64, loop doesn't execute). So with the loop `for (int i = tid; i < 64; i += 96)`: - Warp 0: tid 0..31 -> i = 0..31. 32 loads, coalesced. Writes banks 0..31. - Warp 1: tid 32..63 -> i = 32..63. 32 loads, coalesced. Writes banks 32..63 ≡ 0..31. No conflict (different warps). - Warp 2: tid 64..95 -> i = 64..95, but i>=64 so no work. All threads inactive. Wait, but `tid` in a warp: Warp 0: tid = 0, 1, 2, ..., 31. Warp 1: tid = 32, 33, ..., 63. If block is (24, 4), tid = ty * 24 + tx. Thread (0,0)=0, (1,0)=1, ..., (23,0)=23, (0,1)=24, ..., (23,1)=47, (0,2)=48, ..., (23,2)=71, (0,3)=72, ..., (23,3)=95.
