So i=64..95, but loop exits since i>=64. So warps 0 and 1 each do 32 loads. Warp 2 does nothing. This is fine! And the shared memory writes: Warp 0 writes to s_w[kh][kw][0..31]. Banks 0..31. No conflict. Warp 1 writes to s_w[kh][kw][32..63]. Banks 32..63 ≡ 0..31. No conflict because it's a different warp. Warp 2: no writes. And in the compute phase, threads in a warp access `s_w[kh][kw][oc]`. If all threads in a warp compute the same oc simultaneously, they access the same element (broadcast). No conflict. If threads in a warp compute different oc simultaneously... in our kernel, they all compute the same oc in each iteration of the unrolled loop. Actually, the loop is: ```cpp for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) { accum[oc] += in_val * s_w[kh][kw][oc]; } ``` All threads in the warp access `s_w[kh][kw][oc]` for the SAME oc in the same instruction (broadcast). NO BANK CONFLICTS! This is perfect. One concern: the `__syncthreads()` inside a `#pragma unroll` loop. For kh unrolled over 5 iterations, we'd have 5 barriers. Plus another after the kh loop. Or we can do one barrier after loading all weights. Actually, if we load ALL weights for all 5 kh and 9 kw at once, we only need ONE barrier: ```cpp // Load all weights for current c for (int idx = tid; idx < 5 * 9 * 64; idx += 96) { int oc = idx % 64; int kw = (idx / 64) % 9; int kh = idx / (64 * 9); s_w[kh][kw][oc] = weight[c * (5 * 9 * 64) + kh * (9 * 64) + kw * 64 + oc]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute using loaded weights (no more barriers needed for weights) #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { float in_val = input[...]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) { accum[oc] += in_val * s_w[kh][kw][oc]; } } } ``` But wait, the weight indexing is: `weight[c * (5 * 9 * 64) + kh * (9 * 64) + kw * 64 + oc]`. If we stored the transposed weight as [C_in][KH][KW][C_out], this is contiguous. Now, what about the shared memory bank conflicts during loading with this indexing? `s_w[kh][kw][oc]` where s_w is [5][9][64]. Thread idx writes to: address = ((kh * 9 + kw) * 64 + oc). In a warp, idx values differ by 1 (for tid differing by 1). But `idx` increments by 96 for the loop. So consecutive threads in a warp have idx = tid + k*96. Wait, we iterate `for (int idx = tid; idx < 2880; idx += 96)`. So: Warp 0: tid = 0..31 -> idx = 0..31. For these idx values: idx = kh * 576 + kw * 64 + oc, where kh = idx / 576, kw = (idx / 64) % 9, oc = idx % 64. For idx = 0: kh=0, kw=0, oc=0. For idx = 1: kh=0, kw=0, oc=1. ... For idx = 63: kh=0, kw=0, oc=63. For idx = 64: kh=0, kw=1, oc=0. So for warp 0 (tid 0..31): Thread 0: idx=0 -> oc=0, kw=0, kh=0 -> writes to s_w[0][0][0] (bank 0) Thread 1: idx=1 -> oc=1, kw=0, kh=0 -> writes to s_w[0][0][1] (bank 1) ... Thread 31: idx=31 -> oc=31, kw=0, kh=0 -> writes to s_w[0][0][31] (bank 31) These are 32 consecutive floats in the oc dimension. Banks 0..31. No conflicts! For warp 1 (tid 32..63): Thread 32: idx=32 -> oc=32, kw=0, kh=0 -> writes to s_w[0][0][32] (bank 0, since 32 mod 32 = 0) Thread 33: idx=33 -> oc=33 -> bank 1. ... Thread 63: idx=63 -> oc=63 -> bank 31. Again banks 0..31, but since warp 1 executes separately from warp 0, no conflicts. However, within warp 1, there are also no conflicts (each thread writes to a unique bank). So the loading is completely bank-conflict-free! This is an excellent design. Let me code it up. One more concern: the `idx` computation `int kh = idx / (64 * 9); int kw = (idx / 64) % 9; int oc = idx % 64;` involves division and modulo. For the shared memory write, this is okay because it's inside a loop and there are only ~30 iterations per thread (2880 / 96 = 30). Alternatively, we can unroll the loops or precompute. But the compiler should optimize this. Actually, we can restructure to avoid the modulo inside the loop by having each thread load one (kh, kw) slice at a time: ```cpp for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { int w_base = c * 2880 + kh * 576 + kw * 64; for (int oc = tid; oc < 64; oc += 96) { s_w[kh][kw][oc] = weight[w_base + oc]; } } } ``` This is cleaner! No modulo or division inside the inner loop. And `w_base + oc` is consecutive for consecutive threads. Wait, consecutive threads in a warp have tid values 0..31. They access `w_base + 0`, `w_base + 1`, etc. These are CONSECUTIVE floats. COALESCED! And shared memory writes: `s_w[kh][kw][oc]` for oc = 0..31. Banks 0..31. No conflict. This is perfect! Now for the compute phase, we need to read input values. Let me look at the input access: ```cpp for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { float in_val = input[((b * C_in + c) * H + (h_out + kh)) * W + (w_out + kw)]; } } ``` For threads in a warp: - h_out differs by thread (ty). We established the warp composition: Warp 0: (tx=0..23, ty=0) and (tx=0..7, ty=1) So threads 0..23 have h_out = h_tile*4 + 0. Threads 24..31 have h_out = h_tile*4 + 1. For fixed kh, threads 0..23 read from row h_tile*4 + kh. Threads 24..31 read from row h_tile*4 + 1 + kh. Within each group, w_out = w_tile*24 + tx. So threads read consecutive w values. Global memory access: 24 consecutive floats from one row, and 8 consecutive floats from the next row. Coalesced into 2 transactions (maybe 1 if the hardware is smart and they're close). This is good enough. One more optimization: since H=512 and W=512, and h_out, w_out are known, maybe we can load input into registers first for all kh, kw: ```cpp float in_vals[5][9]; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { in_vals[kh][kw] = input[...]; } } ``` Then in the compute phase: ```cpp #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) { accum[oc] += in_vals[kh][kw] * s_w[kh][kw][oc]; } } } ``` This moves the input loads outside the inner loop, allowing the compiler to better schedule them. And we use 45 registers for `in_vals`. That's fine. Let's do this. Wait, can we fit 45 floats + 64 accum + other registers? 45 + 64 = 109. Plus maybe 15 more for pointers and loop counters. ~124 registers per thread. H100 has 64K per SM. 96 threads * 124 = 11,904. Still well below 64K. And max registers per thread is 255, so 124 is fine. Let me compile this version and see how it goes. Wait, but `__syncthreads()` after loading weights: do we need it if we load all weights first? Yes, because we need to ensure weights are written to shared memory before any thread reads them. So the kernel flow is: 1. `__shared__ float s_w[5][9][64];` 2. `float accum[64];` (initialize) 3. `float in_vals[5][9];` (but this depends on c, so it goes inside the c loop) 4. For c = 0..31: a. Load weights for all (kh, kw, oc) into s_w b. `__syncthreads()` c. Load input for all (kh, kw) into in_vals d. For kh, kw, oc: compute accum[oc] += in_vals[kh][kw] * s_w[kh][kw][oc] 5. Write output Actually, for step 4c, if we load input for all kh, kw first, we have to do it before reading from s_w. That's fine. Let me write it: ```cuda __global__ void __launch_bounds__(96, 12) conv2d_direct_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int KH, int KW, int H_out, int W_out ) { const int TILE_H = 4; const int TILE_W = 24; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int w_tile = blockIdx.x; int h_tile = blockIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; int w_out = w_tile * TILE_W + tx; int h_out = h_tile * TILE_H + ty; if (h_out >= H_out || w_out >= W_out) return; __shared__ float s_w[5][9][64]; float accum[64]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) accum[oc] = 0.0f; int tid = ty * TILE_W + tx; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { // Load all weights for this input channel #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_base = c * (KH * KW * C_out) + kh * (KW * C_out) + kw * C_out; for (int oc = tid; oc < C_out; oc += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { s_w[kh][kw][oc] = weight[w_base + oc]; } } } __syncthreads(); // Load input values for this channel and spatial position int in_base = ((b * C_in + c) * H + h_out) * W + w_out; float in_vals[5][9]; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { in_vals[kh][kw] = input[in_base + kh * W + kw]; } } // Compute #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { float in_val = in_vals[kh][kw]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { accum[oc] += in_val * s_w[kh][kw][oc]; } } } __syncthreads(); } // Store output int out_base = ((b * C_out) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < C_out; ++oc) { output[out_base + oc * H_out * W_out] = accum[oc]; } } ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue. The `__syncthreads()` at the end of the c loop is needed because the next iteration will start writing to s_w, and some threads might still be reading from s_w in the compute phase from the previous iteration. But the compute phase for all threads finishes before any thread reaches the next `__syncthreads()`, so this is fine. Actually, the `__syncthreads()` at the end of the loop is already there. Wait, do we need it? Let's trace: 1. All threads load weights into s_w. 2. `__syncthreads()` ensures all writes are visible. 3. All threads load input into in_vals. 4. All threads read s_w and compute. 5. End of c loop. Next iteration: all threads start loading weights into s_w again. - Do we need a barrier between step 4 and step 5 of the next iteration? - Since all threads execute steps 1-4 before starting the next iteration, and step 1 of the next iteration overwrites s_w, we need to make sure ALL threads have finished step 4 before ANY thread starts step 1 of the next iteration. - The end of the loop body automatically ensures this because it's the same control flow for all threads. Wait, no it doesn't. A warp that finishes step 4 can proceed to the next iteration and start step 1, overwriting s_w, while another warp is still reading s_w from the previous iteration. - So YES, we need `__syncthreads()` at the end of each c loop iteration. Actually, we can place a `__syncthreads()` right after the compute phase and before the weight loading of the next iteration. Currently we have it after the compute phase. Good. Wait, in the code above, the `__syncthreads()` is at the very end of the `c` loop body, after the compute phase. Let me verify: ```cpp for (int c = 0; c < C_in; ++c) { // load weights __syncthreads(); // barrier after weight load // load input // compute __syncthreads(); // barrier after compute, before next weight load } ``` Yes, this is correct. The second `__syncthreads()` ensures all threads finish reading s_w before any thread starts writing to it in the next iteration. Total barriers: 2 per c loop * 32 = 64 barriers. Plus one implicit at the end of the last iteration. This is fine. Now, what about the `in_vals[5][9]` array. It's stored in registers. The compiler should be able to compile this well. Let me think about whether we should use `__launch_bounds__(96, 12)`. The second parameter (minBlocksPerMultiprocessor) is optional and tells the compiler the maximum registers it can use to ensure occupancy. If we leave it out, the compiler calculates registers automatically. With 96 threads and ~120 registers, we can fit about 5 blocks per SM (64K / (96*120) ≈ 5.5). With 5 blocks per SM and 132 SMs, that's 660 concurrent blocks. Total blocks = 21,336. 21,336 / 660 ≈ 32 waves. At 2 GHz with 67 TFLOPS, each wave is ~0.7 microseconds, so 32 waves ≈ 22 microseconds. That might be okay. Actually, wait. If each block does 92,160 FMAs, and we have 660 concurrent blocks, that's 660 * 92,160 = 60.8M FMAs concurrently. At 67 TFLOPS = 67,000 GFLOPS = 67,000M FLOPS, processing 60.8M FMAs at 100% efficiency would take... wait, FMA counts are per block, and blocks execute in parallel. The total theoretical time is Total_FMAs / peak_FLOPS = 37.6 GFLOPs / 67 TFLOPS = 0.56 ms = 560 microseconds. Wait, 37.6 GFLOPs / 67,000 GFLOPS = 0.00056 seconds = 560 microseconds. And with 32 waves, 560 / 32 = 17.5 microseconds per wave. That seems consistent. Can we do better? cuDNN might well achieve 50-70% of peak, giving 400-470 microseconds. If our kernel achieves 30% of peak (due to overhead, barriers, etc.), that's 670 microseconds, which would be slower. But with our very efficient memory access patterns and the fact that we're hardcoded for the exact problem sizes, we might actually do quite well. Let's try and benchmark! Let me write a complete kernel code. I'll also add support for the case where H_out and W_out are not perfectly divisible by tile sizes, but since we know the exact sizes, I can also just hardcode everything. Actually, for flexibility and to avoid out-of-bounds issues, I'll keep the general tile structure with boundary checks. But I know 508/4 = 127 and 504/24 = 21 exactly. Let me write the code now.

reasoning
The error is because the cpp_sources is getting compiled by the C++ compiler which doesn't understand the <<< >>> kernel launch syntax. In load_inline, I should provide a separate .h file or properly structure the code. Actually, the issue is that pybind11 wraps the cpp_sources directly and the C++ compiler tries to compile it. cuda_sources gets compiled separately. But the kernel launch syntax is not valid C++. The proper way: I need to declare the kernel as `extern "C"` in the cuda file and call it via a launcher. Or, I can put the entire thing including kernel launch in the cuda_sources, with a host function wrapper. The cpp_sources should just be declarations or Python-accessible wrappers that don't launch kernels. Let me restructure: put the kernel and host launcher in cuda_sources, and the pybind wrapper in cpp_sources that calls the host launcher function.

reasoning
It compiled. Now let's check correctness. But wait, I need to think about whether the weight layout in the forward is correct. After transposing, weight is [C_in, KH, KW, C_out]. In the kernel, I index weight as: ``` int w_base = c * (KH * KW * C_out) + kh * (KW * C_out) + kw * C_out; ``` So for a given input channel c, kernel position (kh, kw), the C_out weights are contiguous starting at w_base. That matches [C_in, KH, KW, C_out] layout. Good. Next, output writing: ``` int out_base = ((b * C_out) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out; ... output[out_base + oc * H_out * W_out] = accum[oc]; ``` This writes output in channel-major format: [B, C_out, H_out, W_out]. out_base points to (b, 0, h_out, w_out). Then adding oc * H_out * W_out moves to the next output channel. This is correct for NCHW format. But wait, the input layout check: ``` int in_base = ((b * C_in + c) * H + h_out) * W + w_out; ``` This is standard NCHW. Then input[in_base + kh * W + kw] reads (b, c, h_out + kh, w_out + kw). This assumes no dilation and stride 1, which is what we assert. This looks correct. Let's run the correctness check.

Since M_tile positions are flattened from [B, H_out, W_out], they're scattered in the input tensor. Loading them efficiently requires computing their corresponding input locations on the fly. For position m in [0, M), m = b*H_out*W_out + h*W_out + w. The K input features for this position are the elements of the KH x KW stencil across all C_in channels. If K is flattened as [c, kh, kw], then feature k corresponds to input[b, c, h+kh, w+kw]. For K_tile consecutive features, say k_start to k_start+15: These might span across multiple channels and/or kernel positions. Loading them coalesced is hard. For a K_tile of 16, it spans part of a single channel's kernel. For example, K_tile=16 might cover 16 (kh, kw) pairs but there are only 45 per channel. Actually, let me think more carefully. For a fixed channel c and fixed kernel position (kh, kw), all M positions need input[b, c, h+kh, w+kw]. For a tile of 64 consecutive output positions (in the flattened M dimension), these positions might be from different batches and different spatial locations. So input[b, c, h+kh, w+kw] for these 64 positions are scattered and not contiguous. This means loading A tiles efficiently is very hard for flattened M tiling. For 2D spatial tiling, it's better. If I tile the output spatially (say 8x8 positions from the same batch), then for a fixed (kh, kw), the input positions [h+kh, w+kw] form a contiguous-ish block. Specifically: - For a thread block computing [h0:h0+8, w0:w0+8] outputs: - Input needed for position (kh, kw): [h0+kh:h0+kh+8, w0+kw:w0+kw+8] - These are 8 consecutive rows, each with 8 consecutive columns. In memory, row h*W + (w0+kw) to h*W + (w0+kw+7) is contiguous for each row. So for a single (kh, kw), I can load 8x8 = 64 floats per channel with 2 vectorized loads per row (e.g., 2 float4 per row). Not great for coalescing. Actually, 320 floats at 4 bytes each = 1280 bytes. A warp can load this in 10 transactions (128 bytes each). Now let me think about the actual per-thread compute: ```cuda float accum[8]; // 8 output channels for (int c = 0; c < 32; c++) { float in_val[5][9]; // Actually, load from shared memory on demand for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; kw++) { float in = s_input[kh][kw]; // each thread accesses its own offset for (int oc = 0; oc < 8; oc++) { accum[oc] += in * s_weight[kh][kw][oc]; } } } } ``` Wait, each thread has a different position, so it accesses different s_input values. But all threads access the same s_weight values (for their respective output channels). This is actually good: s_input has high access divergence but is cached in shared memory; s_weight has low divergence. For performance, I need to think about bank conflicts: - s_weight: if organized as [oc][kh][kw] or [kh][kw][oc], threads in a warp accessing the same [kh][kw] but different oc will have bank conflicts if oc dimension isn't padded correctly. - Better organization: [kh][kw][oc] with oc padded to avoid bank conflicts. Since 8 channels * 4 bytes = 32 bytes, and banks are 4 bytes wide, 8 consecutive channels occupy 8 consecutive banks. If I access channel oc for thread tx, and tx differs by 1, banks differ by 1. No conflicts! But if threads access the SAME oc, they all hit the same bank. Actually, in the above code, all threads in a warp access the SAME [kh][kw] but DIFFERENT oc. Since oc differs across threads, and the memory layout is [kh][kw][oc], each thread accesses a different address, spaced by sizeof(float). For a warp reading 32 consecutive floats starting at bank 0: - Thread 0 reads bank 0 - Thread 1 reads bank 1 - ... - Thread 31 reads bank 31 (bank 31 mod 32 = 31) No bank conflicts! Great. But wait, each thread only handles 8 output channels, not 32. So in a warp of 32 threads: - Threads 0-3 might handle channel 0-7 - Threads 4-7 might handle channel 0-7 too... no, I said each thread handles 8 channels. For s_weight access at [kh][kw][oc]: - All threads in warp read the SAME [kh][kw], but DIFFERENT oc. Wait no, all threads handle channels 0-7, so they might ALL read oc=0 simultaneously. That would cause a massive bank conflict. Ah, I see the issue. If each thread iterates over oc=0..7 sequentially, then for a given oc, all 32 threads in the warp read s_weight[kh][kw][oc]. Since all threads read the SAME memory address, they all hit the SAME bank. This is a broadcast, which is actually handled efficiently on modern GPUs (no bank conflicts for broadcasts!). But if they read different oc's, and the addresses aren't aligned well, there might be conflicts. Actually, let me reconsider. If all 32 threads read the same address, it's a broadcast and takes 1 cycle (no conflicts). Then they all move to the next oc and broadcast again. This is actually very efficient! So my main concern is the s_input access pattern. Each thread reads its own (h+kh, w+kw) which depends on its spatial position. For thread tx in [0, 7] (W dimension): - Reads s_input[kh][tx + kw] for varying kw - For kw=0: s_input[kh][tx] = banks tx, tx+1, ... (since s_input is 2D) If s_input is organized as [kh][w] with w padded: - Thread 0: bank 0, bank 1, bank 2, ... for kw=0,1,2,... - Thread 1: bank 1, bank 2, bank 3, ... - There are 2-way bank conflicts! To avoid conflicts, I can use a different access pattern or pad the width dimension. But 2-way conflicts are not terrible. Let me pad the width by 1: s_input[kh][w+1]. Then: - Thread 0: bank 0, 1, 2, ... for kw=0,1,2,... - Thread 1: bank 2, 3, 4, ... (shifted by 1 pad + 1 offset = 2) Hmm, this doesn't fully eliminate conflicts. But it's manageable. Actually, for H100, shared memory bank width is 4 bytes (1 float), and there are 32 banks. So for 8 consecutive threads accessing consecutive floats: - Thread 0: bank 0 - Thread 1: bank 1 - ... - Thread 7: bank 7 No conflicts for the same kw. But for different kw values: - Thread 0, kw=0: bank 0 - Thread 1, kw=0: bank 1 - Thread 0, kw=1: bank 1 - Thread 1, kw=1: bank 2 Within a single instruction cycle, if the warp is reading kw for all threads, each thread reads a different bank. If we then increment kw and read again, all threads shift by 1 bank. Still no conflicts! The only issue is if multiple threads try to read the same bank. For a warp of 32 threads with 8 W positions and 4 H positions: - Threads 0-7: W positions 0-7, H position 0 - Threads 8-15: W positions 0-7, H position 1 - ... For kh=0, threads read s_input[0 + h_offset][w_offset + kw]. For threads 0 and 8: - Thread 0: s_input[0][w0 + kw] - Thread 8: s_input[1][w0 + kw] These are in different rows, but the bank is determined by the column index. If both have the same w_offset + kw, they hit the same bank! That's a 2-way conflict. With 4 H positions, it's a 4-way conflict in the worst case. To mitigate this, I can pad the W dimension by an odd number. For example, pad to W+4. Then thread 8 reads from column (w0 + kw + 4), which is bank (w0 + kw + 4) mod 32, while thread 0 reads bank (w0 + kw) mod 32. These differ by 4, so no conflict! Let me use a padding of 4 or 8 for the W dimension in shared memory. OK, that's enough analysis. Let me now write the kernel. Let me write this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, float* output, int B, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int KH, int KW, int H_out, int W_out ) { constexpr int TH = 8; // tile height constexpr int TW = 16; // tile width constexpr int TN = 16; // tile output channels int tx = threadIdx.x; // 0..127 int oc_block = blockIdx.x; // output channel block (0..3 for 64 channels) int w_tile = blockIdx.y; // width tile int h_tile = blockIdx.z % ((H_out + TH - 1) / TH); // height tile int b = blockIdx.z / ((H_out + TH - 1) / TH); // batch int oc_start = oc_block * TN; // 0, 16, 32, 48 int w0 = w_tile * TW; int h0 = h_tile * TH; // Thread mapping: 128 threads -> 8x16 spatial grid int tw = tx % TW; // 0..15 int th = tx / TW; // 0..7 int h_out = h0 + th; int w_out = w0 + tw; // Bounds check if (h_out >= H_out || w_out >= W_out) return; // Shared memory // s_input: [TH+KH][TW+KW+PAD] (12 x 25) // s_weight: [KH][KW][TN] (5 x 9 x 16) extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* s_input = smem; float* s_weight = smem + (TH + KH) * (TW + KW + 4); // pad w by 4 float accum[TN] = {0.0f}; for (int c = 0; c < C_in; c++) { // Load input region for this channel int ih0 = h0; // actually h_out - 0 since we're at top of tile int iw0 = w0; int in_base = ((b * C_in + c) * H + ih0) * W + iw0; // Cooperative load for (int idx = tx; idx < (TH + KH) * (TW + KW + 4); idx += 128) { int local_h = idx / (TW + KW + 4); int local_w = idx % (TW + KW + 4); int ih = ih0 + local_h; int iw = iw0 + local_w; if (local_w < TW + KW && ih < H && iw < W) { s_input[local_h * (TW + KW + 4) + local_w] = input[in_base + local_h * W + local_w]; } else { s_input[local_h * (TW + KW + 4) + local_w] = 0.0f; } } // Load weights for this channel and TN output channels int w_base = (oc_start * C_in + c) * KH * KW; // need to check weight layout for (int idx = tx; idx < KH * KW * TN; idx += 128) { s_weight[idx] = weight[w_base + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; kw++) { float in_val = s_input[(th + kh) * (TW + KW + 4) + (tw + kw)]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < TN; oc++) { accum[oc] += in_val * s_weight[(kh * KW + kw) * TN + oc]; } } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output int out_base = ((b * C_out + oc_start) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out; for (int oc = 0; oc < TN; oc++) { output[out_base + oc * H_out * W_out] = accum[oc]; } } ``` Wait, I need to think about the weight layout. The reference nn.Conv2d uses [C_out, C_in, KH, KW]. I transposed it to [C_in, KH, KW, C_out]. So: - `weight[c, kh, kw, oc]` is the weight for input channel c, output channel oc, at position (kh, kw) - For a block starting at oc_start, the base address is `c * (KH * KW * C_out) + kh * (KW * C_out) + kw * C_out + oc_start` Actually wait, in my earlier code I transposed the weights in __init__: ```python w_t = w.permute(1, 2, 3, 0).contiguous() ``` So the new shape is [C_in, KH, KW, C_out]. For element at [c, kh, kw, oc], the linear index is: `c * KH * KW * C_out + kh * KW * C_out + kw * C_out + oc` So `w_base` for a given c and oc_start should be: `c * KH * KW * C_out + oc_start` (if we iterate over kh, kw in the load) Let me rewrite the weight load: ```cuda for (int idx = tx; idx < KH * KW * TN; idx += 128) { int local_oc = idx % TN; int local_k = idx / TN; int kh = local_k / KW; int kw = local_k % KW; s_weight[idx] = weight[c * KH * KW * C_out + kh * KW * C_out + kw * C_out + oc_start + local_oc]; } ``` This allows coalesced reads because consecutive threads read consecutive output channels for the same kernel position. For the compute, I organized s_weight as [(kh * KW + kw) * TN + oc], which means for fixed (kh, kw), consecutive oc values are consecutive in memory. Now let me think about the output layout. The output is [B, C_out, H_out, W_out]. For out_base = ((b * C_out + oc_start) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out: - output[out_base + oc * H_out * W_out] gives channel (oc_start + oc) at (h_out, w_out) - Consecutive w_out values are consecutive in memory - So for a warp writing the same oc but different w_out, it IS coalesced. But each thread writes all TN channels, which are H_out*W_out apart. If TN=16, that's 16 writes per thread, each 256k floats apart. For a warp, writing oc=0: - Thread 0: out_base - Thread 1: out_base + 1 - ... These are consecutive and coalesced. Then oc=1: - Thread 0: out_base + H_out*W_out - Thread 1: out_base + 1 + H_out*W_out Also consecutive and coalesced. So the write pattern is fine, just 16 coalesced writes per thread. Actually, writing 16 separate coalesced transactions might still have some overhead, but it should be much better than before because we're doing less total work (each spatial position is only computed once, not once per 64 channels). Wait, I just realized: with TN=16, we have 4 blocks per spatial position (for 4 channel groups). Each block writes 16 channels. One concern: all threads accessing the same shared memory address simultaneously might cause some queuing, but on modern GPUs, broadcasts are handled in 1-2 cycles. Another concern: the inner loop of `oc=0..15` with `accum[oc] += in_val * weight_val` has a data dependency on accum[oc]. To improve instruction-level parallelism, I could partially unroll the oc loop: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 16; oc += 4) { accum[oc+0] += in_val * w0; accum[oc+1] += in_val * w1; accum[oc+2] += in_val * w2; accum[oc+3] += in_val * w3; } ``` This allows the compiler to schedule 4 FMAs in parallel. Or I can load the weights into registers first: ```cuda float w[16]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 16; oc++) { w[oc] = s_weight[...]; } ``` Then use w[oc] in the inner loop. The compile succeeded. Now let me run the correctness test. For KH=5, KW=9: `5 * 41 * 4 = 820` bytes. Very small. Let me write the complete kernel. Also, I should double check: the original weight is [C_out, C_in, KH, KW]. I permute it to [C_in, KH, KW, C_out]. In this layout: - `weight[c, kh, kw, oc]` is contiguous in `oc` dimension. - For a fixed (c, kh, kw), the C_out values are contiguous. In my kernel, when I access `weight[w_base_oc + (kh*KW + kw)*C_out]` where `w_base_oc = c*KH*KW*C_out + oc` = `c*KH*KW*C_out + oc + kh*KW*C_out + kw*C_out` = `c*KH*KW*C_out + (kh*KW + kw)*C_out + oc` This is indeed `weight[c, kh, kw, oc]`. Good. Let me compile this version. One more thing: for the shared memory load, I need to make sure all threads participate. With 256 threads and only 200 elements, some threads won't do anything in the loading loop. That's fine. Actually, for the correctness test, the previous kernel had 0 error and passed 5/5. This new kernel should also be correct since it's the same algorithm, just restructured for better memory coalescing. Let me compile it.

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cuda_source = r'''
#include <cuda_runtime.h>

__global__ void __launch_bounds__(256, 2)
conv2d_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ input,
    const float* __restrict__ weight,
    float* __restrict__ output,
    int B, int C_in, int H, int W,
    int C_out, int KH, int KW,
    int H_out, int W_out
) {
    const int WARPS_PER_BLOCK = 8;
    const int WARP_SIZE = 32;
    int warp_id = threadIdx.x / WARP_SIZE;
    int lane = threadIdx.x % WARP_SIZE;

    int total_spatial = B * H_out * W_out;
    int spatial_base = blockIdx.x * WARPS_PER_BLOCK / 2;
    int spatial_idx = spatial_base + (warp_id / 2);

    int s_idx = spatial_idx < total_spatial ? spatial_idx : 0;
    int b = s_idx / (H_out * W_out);
    int rem = s_idx % (H_out * W_out);
    int h = rem / W_out;
    int w = rem % W_out;

    int oc_start = (warp_id % 2) * 32;
    int oc = oc_start + lane;

    float acc = 0.0f;
    int k_max = C_in * KH * KW;

    #pragma unroll 8
    for (int k = 0; k < k_max; k++) {
        int c = k / (KH * KW);
        int rem_k = k % (KH * KW);
        int kh = rem_k / KW;
        int kw = rem_k % KW;

        float in_val = input[((b * C_in + c) * H + (h + kh)) * W + (w + kw)];
        float w_val = weight[k * C_out + oc];
        acc += in_val * w_val;
    }

    if (spatial_idx < total_spatial) {
        int out_idx = ((b * C_out + oc) * H_out + h) * W_out + w;
        output[out_idx] = acc;
    }
}

at::Tensor conv2d_forward_launcher(
    at::Tensor input,
    at::Tensor weight,
    int64_t B, int64_t C_in, int64_t H, int64_t W,
    int64_t C_out, int64_t KH, int64_t KW,
    int64_t H_out, int64_t W_out
) {
    auto output = at::empty({B, C_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options());

    const int WARPS_PER_BLOCK = 8;
    int total_spatial = B * H_out * W_out;
    int grid = (total_spatial + (WARPS_PER_BLOCK / 2) - 1) / (WARPS_PER_BLOCK / 2);

    conv2d_kernel<<<grid, 256>>>(
        input.data_ptr<float>(),
        weight.data_ptr<float>(),
        output.data_ptr<float>(),
        B, C_in, H, W, C_out, KH, KW, H_out, W_out);

    return output;
}
'''

cpp_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>

at::Tensor conv2d_forward_launcher(
    at::Tensor input,
    at::Tensor weight,
    int64_t B, int64_t C_in, int64_t H, int64_t W,
    int64_t C_out, int64_t KH, int64_t KW,
    int64_t H_out, int64_t W_out
);

torch::Tensor conv2d_forward(
    torch::Tensor input,
    torch::Tensor weight,
    int64_t B, int64_t C_in, int64_t H, int64_t W,
    int64_t C_out, int64_t KH, int64_t KW,
    int64_t H_out, int64_t W_out
) {
    return conv2d_forward_launcher(input, weight, B, C_in, H, W, C_out, KH, KW, H_out, W_out);
}
'''

cap = torch.cuda.get_device_capability()
arch_flag = f"-arch=sm_{cap[0]}{cap[1]}"

conv2d_opt = load_inline(
    name="conv2d_opt_final",
    cpp_sources=cpp_source,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=["conv2d_forward"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=[arch_flag, "-O3", "--use_fast_math"],
    verbose=False,
)


class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, groups=1, bias=False):
        super(ModelNew, self).__init__()
        self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=groups, bias=bias)
        with torch.no_grad():
            w = self.conv2d.weight
            w_t = w.permute(1, 2, 3, 0).contiguous()
            self.conv2d.weight.data = w_t
            self._transposed = True

    def forward(self, x):
        B, C, H, W = x.shape
        weight = self.conv2d.weight
        C_out = weight.shape[3]
        KH = weight.shape[1]
        KW = weight.shape[2]
        H_out = H - KH + 1
        W_out = W - KW + 1
        return conv2d_opt.conv2d_forward(x, weight, B, C, H, W, C_out, KH, KW, H_out, W_out)
